BRAZIL
SECOND PROGRAMMATIC FINANCIAL SECTOR ADJusTMENT LOAN
LOAN AND PROGRAM SUMMARY
Borrower:            Federative Republic of Brazil
Implementing         Central Bank of Brazil and Brazilian Securities and Exchange
Agencies:            Commission
Poverty Category:    Not Applicable.
Amount:              US$404.04 million.
Terms:               Fixed spread loan in US dollars, with a bullet repayment in ten years.
Commitment Fee:      0.85% per annum during the first four years and 0.75% per annum
thereafter on undisbursed loan amounts, beginning to accrue sixty days
after the loan agreement is signed.
Front-End Fee        1% of the loan amount, to be financed under the loan.
Objective:           The objective of the proposed operation is to lend continued support to
the comprehensive financial sector reform program pursued by the
authorities and supported by the Bank through a first programmatic
loan in 2001, and its recently expanded sectoral reform agenda.
Through its support for a strengthened financial system, the loan would
help to anchor poverty reduction in Brazil with sustainable, sound and
crisis-dampening economic growth, as well as directly aiding
poverty alleviation through enhanced access to rmancial services
Description:         The proposed second financial sector adjustment loan supports
sustained progress in all reform areas covered in the first programmatic
operation, and gives particular recognition to accelerated
implementation of major and difficult financial sector reforms, beyond
the scope of the first programmatic adjustment operation. Areas
covered include:
(i) Progress with regard to program actions to:
* Enhance access to and raise efficiency in financial intermediation;
*  Promote banking system soundness and preventfinancial crises;
* Improve governance in capital markets and strengthen the regulator
* Reduce systemic payments risks and Central Bank exposure to
losses in settlement.
(ii) Public bank reform, encompassing initiation of major
restructuring / reform of Federal banks, plus extension of
privatization of state-owned public banks, including:
* Restoration to health offederal bank balance sheets, recognizing
and absorbing past losses
* Assumption by government of risks and costs incurred due to
Page vii



future implementation of government programs and the
unbundling of government and commercial objectives
• Establishment of inproved internal controls, and
* Preparation of blueprint for second stage reforms
(iii) Passage / submission of major legal reforms pertaining to the
financial sector, including notably passage of a landmark revision of
the Corporate Law, which enhances minority shareholder rights, and
passage of a revised CVM law, strengthening the securities regulator.
A Constitutional amendment which would unblock legislation in several
areas, including the submission to Congress of a Central Bank law is
now before Congress. This is not a part of the core package for the
present operation.
Benefits             Brazil's financial sector reform program will help to finnly anchor
sustainable, environmentally and socially sound growth with
directly targeted poverty reduction through measures to expand
access to financial services, and enhance protection against crises.
The program rests upon dialogue of unusual quality at the highest
levels and enables the building of a partnership in reform efforts with a
key player whose financial sector has systemic regional links.
Specifically, benefits of the overall program include (i) broadened
access to a wider spectrum of financial services by the poor and
measures to increase efficiency and transparency of overall financial
intermediation; (ii) sounder operations of both public and private banks
(iii) reduced systemic risk and Central Bank exposure to risk of loss in
settlement, and (iv) increased securities market participation and hence
enhanced access to capital for enterprise growth. Expected benefits of
public bank reform include enhanced financial soundness in Brazil's
largest financial institutions; enhanced fiscal transparency and the
removal of distortions in financial markets. Benefits of progress with
fundamental financial sector legislation include significant
improvements in the governance framework for corporations,
strengthening shareholder rights and encouraging increased
participation in Brazil's stock markets, as well as strengthened
securities regulation.
If the hoped for passage of a fundamental Constitutional amendment
occurs it would enable the rationalization of a series of areas in the
financial system.
Risks                (i) Political Uncertainty: The comprehensiveness and pace of the
reform program so far has been sustained by the well-coordinated
current economic team, working collaboratively across the Central
Bank, Ministry of Finance and regulatory agencies, and the support this
team has enjoyed from the Office of the President. The outlook after
the elections would depend upon the new administration. These
Page viii



uncertainties are exacerbated by the practice of political appointment of
key senior financial sector officials including the head of the Central
Bank and federal financial institutions. The drive of the present
administration to firmly entrench changes prior to the end of their term
and their success in building broad-based public support for reform are
mitigating factors.
(ii) Adverse Macroeconomic Circumstances - domestic and
international: As in 2001, Brazil remains potentially vulnerable to
external shocks and weakened investor confidence in the region which
could impact adversely on the exchange rate and weaken macro
conditions. In late 2001, Brazil restored macroeconomic stability, and
investors have substantially 'decoupled' Brazil from the contagion of
Argentina. But Brazil will need to maintain fiscal discipline in order to
ensure debt sustainability. The government has clearly reiterated its
commitment to sound fiscal policy through a number of measures,
including a recent federal budget cut of US$5.3 billion. The proposed
operation would remain as relevant in case of turbulence and could
further help to anchor markets.
(iii) Implementation difficulties in sustaining future stages of
federal bank reform: Implementation of the next stage of reforms for
the federal banks depends on the adoption of a shared vision by the
banks themselves, the government and the Central Bank. Difficult
decisions will arise regarding business choices, and the government in
its dual capacity as owner of the federal banks and provider of support
for policy based lending could be in a conflicted position. Moreover,
senior managers both at the federal banks and the Central Bank may
change in the upcoming elections. However, the institutionalization of
enhanced supervision of these banks, uniform prudential regulations,
together with the adoption of clear strategic plans by the institutions
themselves will help to maintain the momentum of reform.
Financing Plan       Not Applicable
Net Present Value    Not Applicable

INTERNATIONAL BANK FOR RECONSTRUCTION AND DEVELOPMENT
PROGRAM DOCUMENT
FOR A SECOND PROGRAMMATIC FINANCIAL SECTOR ADJUSTMENT LOAN
TO THE
FEDERATIVE REPUBLIC OF BRAZIL
I. INTRODUCTION
1.    This memorandum proposes a Single-Tranche Second Programmatic Financial Sector
Adjustment Loan (Second PFSECAL) to the Federative Republic of Brazil in an amount of
US$404.04 million. The Loan would support an extensive program of financial sector reforms.
The present Loan follows a previous, first, programmatic financial sector adjustment loan to
Brazil, in the amount of US$404.04 million, approved by the Bank's Board on May 24, 2001
(Loan No.7053-BR), followed by a US$14.5 million companion Financial Sector Technical
Assistance Loan FSTAL (Loan No. 4637-BR), approved by the Bank's Board on September 4,
2001, intended to support the implementation of the broad-based programmatic reform through
technical assistance to Brazil's two key financial regulatory agencies.
BANK FINANCIAL SECTOR DIALOGUE AND OPERATIONS IN BRAZIL
2.    The Bank's first programmatic adjustment loan to Brazil's financial sector had its
underpinnings in a series of financial sector reform actions initiated with the stabilization
brought about by the 1994 Real Plan, which accelerated after 1999, when macroeconomic
stabilization was demonstrated to endure despite severe exchange rate turbulence. The program
included under the loan was the culmination of Bank dialogue with financial sector authorities
initiated through a technical assistance loan to the Central Bank in 1997, and subsequently
expanded and deepened by a series of targeted policy notes, more comprehensive studies and
continued interchange of views and consultative dialogue.
3.    At the time of the first loan, programmatic support for financial sector reforms was
envisaged for a total amount of up to US$1.2 billion, spread over two to three loans, with timing
depending on the pace and depth of future reform programs. Although macroeconomic
circumstances confronting the country grew more difficult in 2001, due to both a deterioration in
external conditions and a domestic energy crisis, the government has not only maintained but
accelerated its pace of financial sector reforms. CAS discussions in 2001 reconfirmed the
financial sector as a priority area for assistance through programmatic adjustment lending.
4.    The first programmatic loan rested upon a series of reform achievements which had
occurred cumulatively over the period since 1999 (detailed in Part V of this report). Future
programmatic actions supported by the first loan focused on reforms that already enjoyed broad
political support and were deemed to be within the implementation capacity of the executive
branch. A careful distinction was thus made in the first operation between areas of reform which
were supported by the Bank program and the wider reform agenda of the government with



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
regard to the financial system. The latter included ambitious and difficult reforms such as
constitutional amendments, needed to permit fundamental financial sector legislation, as well as
the restructuring of the federal banks and the privatization of state-owned banks. Given the
difficulty of predicting the timing of their realization due to the need for political support and in
some cases, Congressional approval, such efforts were recognized by the Bank but omitted from
the subset of actions under the Bank-supported programmatic loan agenda. Bank dialogue with
the government in these areas, at the time of the first operation, was limited.
RECENT REFoRM ACHIEVEMENTS - BASIS OF SECOND PROGRAMMATIC FINANCIAL SECTOR LOAN
5.    Although less than a year from the presentation of the first programmatic financial sector
loan to the Bank's Board, there have been major achievements in specific key areas of the
financial sector, and substantial progress on a broad number of fronts. Particularly noteworthy
are the acceleration of steps taken towards initiating the reform of the Federal banks,
consolidation and extension of restructuring and privatization of state-owned public banks, and
progress in major legal reforms, including the passage of a landmark revision of the Corporate
Law. Federal Bank reform was beyond the scope of the first Bank-supported financial sector
program due to political sensitivity and uncertainty surrounding its likelihood. Moreover
dialogue between the Bank and the authorities in this areas has opened up, in terms of discussion
of the potential future evolution of reforms from both a sectoral and institutional perspective.
There has been broad parallel progress on all major fronts with regard to achievement of
indicative actions for a second programmatic loan, as envisaged in the first operation and
described above. Achievements in some cases exceed expectations. These areas of action
together form the pillars of the proposed second financial sector adjustment operation. Further, a
Constitutional amendment regarding fundamental financial sector legislation has progressed
through Congress. Its passage would open the way for submission to Congress of a new Central
Bank law. As a caveat, however, it should be added that notwithstanding the solid progress made
on programmatic actions, there is still a substantial unfinished agenda ahead, looking at overall
financial sector reform needs. The present operation should be viewed as an important phase in
Brazil's long path towards reform and achievement of best practices in its financial system.
PROPOSED SECOND PROGRAMMATIC FINANCIAL SECTOR ADJUSTMENT OPERATION - OBJECTIVES
AND SCOPE
6.    The proposed second financial sector adjustment operation would lend continued support
to the broad-based and comprehensive financial sector reform program pursued by the authorities
and supported by the Bank through a first programmatic loan in 2001, encompassing issues of
expanded access and increased efficiency of financial intermediation, increased bank soundness,
reduction of payments system risk and greater minority participation in capital markets. The
loan would give particular recognition to sustained progress achieved with all programmatic
reform areas covered in the first operation, and to the acceleration in implementation of difficult
reforms concerning federal banks which were beyond the scope of the first operation. Through
its support for a strengthened and environmentally sound financial system, the loan would help
to anchor poverty reduction in Brazil with sustained and crisis-free economic growth, thus aiding
the long term alleviation of poverty. The ongoing and comprehensive reform program of the
Page 2



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
government, supported by the Bank, is presented in matrix form in Annex 1. Over the medium
term, the Program's goals are:
* Separating government development policy from commercial banking;
* Enhancing transparency and financial soundness of both public and private banks;
*  Broadening access to financial services, especially for the poor;
*  Increasing efficiency and depth of financial intermediation; and
* Rationalizing financial sector legislation and regulation
7.    Based on this framework, reforms supported by the present loan comprise the following
broad areas:
(i) Broad progress on virtually all major fronts as envisaged in the first operation.
8.    Especially noteworthy are significant landmark changes that improve access to
underserved markets as more fully described in paragraphs 25 to 30 below. Equally important
are improvements in the process of financial intermediation dealing with contract enforcement
and risk mitigation. On the other side of the ledger, one measures of efficiency, spreads, have
been stubbornly high, due to a variety of factors including macro phenomena (described further
in paras. 47-50). Reforms supported by the first programmatic loan comprised actions to (i)
increase access to and raise efficiency in financial intermediation; (ii) promote the soundness of
the banking system and its defenses against financial crisis by enhancing the effectiveness of
bank regulation and supervision and improving bank failure resolution, the deposit insurance
system, and contingency planning; (iii) enhance transparency, promoting good governance, and
strengthening the regulator in securities markets; and (iv) protect the safety and soundness of the
financial system through payments systems reforms intended to reduce systemic risks and
safeguard the Central Bank from exposure to loss in the settlement of payments (for details see
Report No. P-7448-BR of April 26, 2001 proposing the First Programmatic Financial Sector
Adjustment Loan).Within the Bank program envisaged in the first operation, there is progress in
all major areas (detailed in Part V of this report).
(ii) Public bank recapitalization, restructuring and privatization, encompassing the
difficult initiation of reform and restructuring of the Federal banks, as well as extension
and consolidation of restructuring and privatization of state-owned public banks. First
stage reforms at Federal banks include the restoration to health of their balance sheets,
recognition of losses incurred due to the implementation of government mandated
programs and establishment of a mechanism for transparent budgetary payment for any
required future transfers, permitting the unbundling of government and commercial
objectives. A second follow up stage of reform is underway which envisages the adoption
of strategic plans to raise efficiency and profitability, strengthen internal governance and
improve internal cost accountability, information systems and controls.
9.    In June 2001 the federal government took the unprecedented step of announcing
major steps towards the clean up of the balance sheets of federal banks, recognizing losses
accumulated for years in their portfolios, partially due to credits extended at the behest of the
Page 3



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
Federal government. These measures followed the first rigorous inspection of these institutions
by Central Bank supervisors, under its new Global Consolidated Inspection program. Balance
sheet restructuring will now bring the banks into compliance with the Central Bank regulations
on capital adequacy as well as a series of recently issued prudential guidelines including loan
classification and provisioning, public sector lending and market risk. The loan would also give
credit for the near completion of the state and municipal bank restructuring and privatization
program, established under the PROES program, which required states and municipalities whose
banks were largely insolvent to federalize, restructure, privatize, transform or liquidate such
institutions. The program has been applied to 44 out of the 47 state-owned banks that existed at
the inception of PROES. Of these 44, 10 have been liquidated, 10 privatized (including the
landmark privatization of Banespa, the very large state bank of Sao Paulo, auctioned end-2000,)
5 restructured, and 16 converted into development agencies. After a gap of nearly a year,
privatizations have accelerated again, with another three privatizations in November 2001,
December 2001 and January 2002. Four remaining privatizations are under preparation (see
Annex 2), and a fifth state owned institution has also applied for inclusion in the privatization
program in 2002.
(iii) Progress in legal reforms pertaining to the financial sector, including the passage
of a landmark revision of the Corporate Law enhancing governance in publicly held
enterprises and thus encouraging share ownership.
10.   The long-awaited reforms of the Corporate law were finally approved by Congress on
September l9th and signed by the President on October 31st. An accompanying bill to enhance
the independence of the Securities Commission was also passed, as a Decree Law, at the same
time (and subsequently converted into Law 10411 on February 26, 2002). The passage of these
laws marks a major improvement in the access to capital market participation for minority
shareholders, and hence should lead to a deepening of Brazil's capital markets and availability
of capital for the enterprise sector. Amendments to the CVM law increase the independence of
the CVM and strengthen the regulatory framework for securities markets.
11.   Meanwhile, an amendment to Article 192 of the Constitution has progressed further
through Congress and its approval is awaited. At present the Constitution requires the financial
system to be governed by a single law, thus precluding the possibility of independent legislative
initiatives regarding various financial system regulators. The amendment was approved by the
Senate and by specialized congressional committees in the summer of 2001. The full House vote
is awaited in 2002. Following the expected passage of this amendment, a draft new Central Bank
law will be presented to Congress.
IMF RELATIONS AND THE JOiNT FSAP
12.   The IMF has supported Brazil's overall adjustment program since 1998 under two stand-
by agreements, the most recent of which was signed in September 2001 and is due to expire in
December 2002. Brazil has been successfully implementing this program to generate primary
surpluses that are consistent with a sustainable public sector net debt path. Brazil's fiscal
performance exceeded its public sector primary surpluses targets of 3.1 percent, 3.25 percent,
and 3.35 percent of GDP respectively for 1999, 2000, and 2001. The target for 2002 is 3.5
Page 4



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
percent and is expected to be met. These targets provide the overall fiscal framework for the
proposed PFSECAL.
13.   Throughout the implementation of the IMF stand-by agreements, the Bank and
Fund are in regular consultations on the adjustment program, and there is close
collaboration.' The Bank will continue to monitor macroeconomic policies in parallel, including
fiscal policies and management, together with the authorities and in coordination with the IMF.
It is expected that early identification of possible areas of concern will lead to corrective actions
by the Government, as appropriate, thus ensuring a satisfactory macroeconomic situation for the
effective implementation of the program supported by the PFSECAL.
14.   During the first phase of the financial sector reform program and in the early stages of the
second phase, the IMF supported the Government on macroeconomic policies. The Bank,
building on ongoing support to the authorities through Technical Assistance lending and non-
lending services, has supported the Government on structural sector reforms, including
those under the proposed Loan. In particular, the Bank has supported the Central Bank over an
extended period of time in improving banking supervision and strengthening the banking safety
net. Bank assistance will be extended through the Bank's new Technical Assistance lending to
Brazil's securities regulator and to the non-bank sector.
15.   More active collaboration in the financial sector is now growing. The 2000 Basle
Core Principle Report on banking supervision was jointly prepared. And at the request of the
government, a major Financial Sector Assessment of Brazil was initiated in March 2002, and is
continuing over the summer of 2002, jointly staffed by the IMF and the Bank. The exhaustive
assessment of vulnerabilities undertaken in the FSAP could valuably contribute to the eventual
design of a third phase operation.

17.   Consultations have been maintained with the IDB, in the area of their financial sector

preparing a grant of around US$2m in technical assistance for Brazil's securities commission,
the CVM.
H. LINKS TO POVERTY REDUCTION
BRAZIL's OVERALL TARGETS AND ACHIEVEMENTS IN POVERTY REDUCTION
18.   The Government remains unwavering in its commitments to the objective of reducing
Brazil's rate of extreme poverty by 50 percent by the year 2015, recognizing that essential
prerequisites for the achievement of poverty targets are fiscal stabilization, macroeconomic
stability and the achievement of broad-based growth, combined with appropriately targeted
social policies and increased participation of the poorest segments in public decisions. World
Bank analysis shows that the Govemment's poverty reduction goal can be achieved through a
combination of 4.5 percent annual growth, continued increases in education, and further
improvements in other social policies. The government's positive achievements in short-term
measures of poverty during 1999 and 2000 are consistent with good growth performance during
this period. Equally, the sustained achievement of poverty reduction in the present period will
depend critically on continued macroeconomic stability.
19.   Targeted strategies and social policies for poverty reduction span a number of areas;
investments in the poor through better education, health as well as improved access to physical
capital through land reform, enhanced access to credit for small entrepreneurs and small farmers,
and enhanced social protection including social assistance and transfer payments. World Bank
financed projects support several elements of this poverty reduction strategy, backed by the
Bank's program of policy advisory work. Two large reports on urban as well as rural poverty in
Brazil were completed over 2001, and ongoing work for state economic memoranda in the
Page 6



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
poorer northeastern states of Piauf, Maranhao, Rio Grande do Norte and Ceara will include
investigations of state level actions for targeted poverty reduction.
DIRECT AND INDIRECT IMPACT ON POVERTY REDUCTION OF FINANCIAL STRENGTH AND STABILITY
20.    The case for poverty reduction through financial sector strengthening rests upon a series
of factors. First of all, expected reforms would have a positive impact on economic growth,
which is one of the largest single factors impacting upon poverty reduction. As discussed in
detail in documentation for the first financial sector programmatic adjustment loan to Brazil,
there is a large body of empirical evidence demonstrating positive links between financial depth
and soundness and economic growth2, and Brazil-specific historical evidence on an inverse
relation between growth and poverty.3 Much recent international evidence shows that the poor
fully participate in growth, in most countries, although the extent may be affected by the initial
distribution of wealth.
21.    Second, a strengthened financial system, as supported by the proposed loan, will impact
upon poverty reduction through the reduction of volatility and provision of a cushion
against economic crises. As argued at the commencement of the present series of programmatic
loans for Brazil's financial systems, financial volatility impacts particularly negatively upon
poverty because (i) shallow domestic financial markets magnify international credit and output
slowdowns, impacting especially adversely on the poor, who are the least able to withstand even
small fluctuations in income and savings. (ii) In addition, financial crises typically entail huge
fiscal costs that crowded out social spending. In the case of Brazil, the crisis of 1995 had a fiscal
cost of about 13 percent of GDP.4 (iii) Financial crises raise the risk of a return to inflationary
finance, a form of taxation that is typically borne disproportionately by those in lower income
brackets. (iv) At a more basic level, major crises can lead to serious financial system
disintermediation, capital flight and an erosion of confidence which can be a setback to growth
2 World Bank Report No. P7448-BR; April 26,2001, on the First Programmatic Financial Sector Adjustment Loan.
A recent exhaustive survey of literature on this issue may be found in Paul Holden and Vassili Prokopenko, 2001
'Financial Development and Poverty Alleviation: Issues and Policy Implications for Developing and Transition
Countries'. IMF working Paper WP/01/160. Bank studies demonstrating links between financial depth and
soundness and economic growth include Robert G. King and Ross Levine, 1993, Finance and Growth: Schumpeter
Might be Right. World Bank, Policy Research Working Paper 1083; and Thorsten Beck, Ross Levine, and Norman
Loayza, 1999, 'Finance and the Sources of Growth', World Bank, Policy Research Working Paper 2057.
3 As cited in the first programmatic loan, the impact of growth on poverty reduction is supported by extensive
research, including Gallup, John Luke; Radelet, Steven; and Warner, Andrew, "Economic Growth and the Income of
the Poor", Harvard Institute for International Development, November 1998; Roemer, Michael and Gugerty, Mary
Kay, "Does Economic Growth Reduce Poverty", Harvard Institute for International Development, March 1997.
International evidence examined in David Dollar and Aart Kraay, (2000) "Growth is Goodfor the Poor"
demonstrates that the poor benefit from growth, a finding also endorsed by the recent World Development Report
(2001)
4Fiscal costs of banking crises have been very large in several countries, ranging from about 4 percent of GDP in
the year of the crises to as much as 50 percent in the 1999 Indonesia crisis. See World Bank, Finance for Growth,
December 20, 2000 and Honohan and Klingebiel, Controlling the Fiscal Costs of Banking Crisis, September 2000
and Caprio and Klingebiel, Bank Insolvencies: Cross Country Experience, July 1996.
Page 7



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
for years.5 Recent events in Argentina and the heightened possibility' of regional contagion of
crises in confidence emphasize the need to protect against external instability.
22.    Third, in addition to factors discussed in the first programmatic loan, appropriately
framed financial sector policies, particularly those affecting the poorer segments and small
and medium enterprises can also help to directly alleviate poverty through enhanced
employment and income generating activities for the poor. A recent study of 15 Latin American
countries using household survey data demonstrates that there is a huge presence of the poor in
microenterprises. The potential impact of better access to credit in Brazil is large: about 95
percent of registered firms in Brazil are enterprises with less than 100 workers and account for
about 46 percent of registered workers. Financial sector policies which are targeted specifically
at creating an enabling environment for lending to this segment, including areas such as credit
registries, credit bureaus and a modernized legal environment for micro credit, can directly
impact the reduction of inequality and poverty.6
POVERTY REDUCTION IN THE PROPOSED PROGRAM - EXPANDED ACCESS
23.    The government's financial sector reform program supported by the Bank would
positively affect poverty reduction through all three channels discussed above. Growth
inducing effects of the proposed program stem from many sources. Federal bank reform
measures require the removal of credit risk from certain operations managed by deposit taking
Federal banks (including so-called 'constitutional funds' and rural credit) as part of the
government's development policies and the introduction of fiscal transparency so that future
operations which carry out special policy mandates through the budgetary process. These
measures will lead to the containment of contingent fiscal liabilities and hence better fiscal
management and greater macro stability which is a growth prerequisite. The programmatic loan
also supports measures to increase intermediation efficiency through the reduction of costs of
intermediation, lowering of bank spreads through increased credit risk information and stronger
legal instruments to enforce credit contracts and reduce credit risk. It also supports reduced
payments risks, deeper capital markets and better banking supervision all of which will impact
positively upon the efficiency and cost of financial intermediation thus increasing real sector
productivity, fostering increased financial deepening, and hence growth.
24.    Enhanced and deeply entrenched protection against crises would be provided by several
interlocking elements of the program. First of all, several measures embodied in the first
programmatic loan address the reduction of the financial system's vulnerability to crises, in
particular, the ongoing strengthening of financial supervision, the improvements in the legal
framework for bankruptcy and bank failure resolution, and the launching of crisis contingency
5 It has been demonstrated that crises in Venezuela (1994), Mexico (1995), and Ecuador (1998) significantly
increased the incidence of poverty. See David De Ferranti and Guillermo Perry, Securing our Future in a Global
Economy (2000), Chapter 5, p. 78.
6 Glenn D. Westley (October 2001) 'Can Financial Market Policies Reduce Income Inequality?' Inter-American
Development Bank, Washington DC. These issues are being investigated in detail in an ongoing AAA task on
Access to Financial Services in Brazil, underway in the Bank's LCSFF group, and parallel studies are also being
undertaken on Mexico and Colombia, for the urban poor (see Annex 3).
Page 8



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
planning mechanisms. Second, a major new element is added in the present operation through
reforms underway at the public banks which have provided for the first time for the application
of standard prudential norms to such institutions, including requirements for capital adequacy
and loan loss provisioning, and the restoration to health of their balance sheets in accordance
with these norms. These measures have hugely reduced government vulnerability to potential
crises due to the lack of financial soundness of these largest of Brazil's financial institutions.
Given the fundamental role of these federal financial institutions in providing services to the
underserved segments (see Annex 3 for details), their financial strength provides a bulwark
against poverty impacts of potential financial crises. Finally, if achieved, the ambitious reform of
the amendment of Article 192 of the Constitution would pave the way for fundamental
rationalization of financial sector oversight. The consequent submission to Congress of the
Central Bank law would embody the first among a series of potential streamlinings of the
regulatory institutions, thus enhancing systemic oversight capacity.
25.   Apart from such indirect impacts upon poverty reduction, a new emphasis has been built
into the proposed second programmatic loan on measures that directly attack poverty
reduction through enhanced access to financial services for the poor and underserved segments
of the population. An in-depth dialogue has begun with authorities in Brazil on measures
pertaining to access to financial services, supported by a broad-based Bank analytical and
advisory activity (AAA) on this topic, which will help the government to investigate household
levels of access differentiated by income level, institutional participation in access, and the
policy infrastructure which differentially affects the access to financial services. Significant
ongoing work at the Central Bank, and in external institutions has been shared with the Bank and
incorporated in the program.
26.   A series of recent measures have been launched by the government to expand access
which are supported by the programmatic adjustment agenda.7 These include first, the special
investigation of issues relating to the expansion of microcredit in Brazil by the Conselho da
Comunidade Soliddria.8 Included among its four commissions on microfinance was a working
group under the leadership of the Central Bank which examined the legal framework for
microcredit. The Action Plan proposed by this group formed the basis for the revisions
made in the microfinance framework in July 2001, including permission to microfinance
enterprises to use lines of credit from financial institutions, operate nationwide, and set up
service outposts.9 The overall study prepared by the conmnissions and their key findings were
summarized in a document entitled "The Expansion of Microcredit in Brazil" (A Expansdo do
Microcr,Jdito no Brasil), released in October 2001.
27.   Second, Brazil took the bold step this year of auctioning of the postal franchise to the
banking sector in August 2001, in order to enable the extension of the network of provision

of financial services, through a national chain of financial service correspondent outposts. It was

Brasileira de Correios e Telegrafos). By the end of 2001, 1,000 post office agencies were

access via a survey of the needs of small and microenterprises, known as the Business Climate
survey (FACS), which will be coordinated by DEC, in close collaboration with the small
enterprise department of the IFC. The survey includes a substantial module on access to financial
services for microenterprises.
29.   A fourth significant development in the expansion of services for savers is the
government's new program to allow individual small savers to purchase government
bonds, through the internet, under its new Treasury Direct program. The initiative is pioneering
in Latin America, and has the double advantage of allowing a safe savings vehicle for small
savers, remunerated at market rates, while at the same time, allowing the government more
efficient mechanisms for tapping local debt markets.10 Issues began on January 7, 2002. Prior to
this, only banks and bond dealers with a minimum amount of R$50,000 could purchase
government bonds. The new scheme permits small investors with as little as R$200, or less
than US$100, to buy government bonds. Data on sales till early April indicate that the program
is achieving its goal of tapping small savings; a third of investors involved have invested less
than R$1,000, and almost two thirds of all investors have invested sums of below R$5,000.
30.   To boost competition, strengthen consumer rights, and improve disclosure, financial
institutions must provide more detailed account information to their clients, from April
2001. This includes information filed for the past two years including credit history, financing
operations, and average monthly balances. And to improve transparency on overdrafts accounts,
commercial banks must disclose more detailed information about financial fees and the amounts
charged on overdraft in monthly statements. Overall principles for fostering additional
transparency, discipline, competition and reliability on the part of financial institutions have been
spelled out in Banking Consumer Protection rules, which consolidate these principles."1

31.   In addition it should be pointed out that a number of actions in the overall program which
are also aimed at increasing efficiency of intermediation and strengthening financial institutions
also aim to foster and support expanded access. Examples are the measures taken to enhance the
information provided on small borrowers by the Credit Risk data base of the Central Bank,
measures taken to enhance secondary markets for housing through the introduction of
instruments for securitization of real estate credits, the Cedula de Credito Imobilidrio - CCI and
related measures.12 These would make the supply of credit larger, cheaper, and more available to
lower-income households and to micro, small and medium enterprises. And key actions in areas
such as securities markets, in the form of the passage of the Corporate and CVM laws, also
have their underpinnings in improved participation of minority shareholders in Brazil's
stock markets, i.e., increased access to capital markets for such persons.
III. RECENT MACROECONOMIC DEVELOPMENTS
32.   Starting in the early nineties, Brazil has adopted a comprehensive package of
economic reforms that has fundamentally transformed its economy. The reform package,
implemented in different stages, covered diverse areas ranging from trade and monetary
management to exchange rate policy and the creation of fiscal institutions that enhanced
credibility in the sustainability of public finances. The first stage, until 1992, involved trade
liberalization with average tariffs being lowered from 39 to 15 percent, flexibilization of capital
account transactions and establishment of the regional trade agreement, Mercosul. A second
stage of reform began with the Real Plan in 1994 and concentrated on monetary stabilization
with the most striking development being the reduction of inflation rates of the order of 5000
percent in mid-1994 to 6 percent in 2000. This reform stage also involved constitutional changes
that reduced earmarked expenditures and allowed fiscal policy to regain some flexibility. These
changes, plus a favorable external environment, brought about the end of chronic and high
inflation and an average annual growth rate of 4.2 percent in the 1992-1997 period.
Simultaneously, demand for foreign savings increased as the current account deficit went from
zero in the period 1990-1994 to a deficit of 4.2 percent of GDP in 1998. Following monetary
stabilization in 1994, the authorities undertook a series of further reforms including a large
privatization program and stabilization of the financial system.
33.   To reduce the vulnerability to external shocks and to ensure a sustainable debt path,
Brazil has, since 1998, undertaken ambitious and credible fiscal adjustment. Fiscal
adjustment lagged behind other elements of the reform program initially, but was implemented in
earnest after the Russia crisis in late 1998. Supported by an IMF program, the Government has
since complied with quarterly fiscal targets and consistently generated a consolidated primary
fiscal surplus of more than 3 percent of GDP. Government estimates suggest that the nominal
deficit in the twelve months until March 2002 amounted to 4.25 % of GDP. At the same time,
the institutional framework for fiscal management has been improved. In early 1999 authorities
in Brazil adopted a flexible exchange rate and an inflation-targeting framework. Among the
larger countries, only Mexico and Chile register larger cumulative growth since 1999, though
2Discussed further below in the section on credit contracts and their enforcement.
Page I 1



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
their volatility has also been large. Growth prospects have been enhanced due to reduced
vulnerability to both external and domestic shocks, achieved by smaller trade deficits and the
elimination of primary public sector deficits. The key elements to achieve these outcomes have
been the adoption of the floating exchange rate regime, the credible and permanent reduction in
the inflation rate, the tax increases, strict austerity of those expenditures directly under
governmental control and the adoption of the Fiscal Responsibility Law that supported
adjustment processes, especially at the sub national level.
34.   The positive outlook for Brazil that prevailed in early 2001 was dampened by the
international slowdown, recent events in Argentina, and a domestic energy crisis. After
growth of 4.5 percent in 2000, a similar performance was expected for 2001. Then intemational
demand began to soften as the result of the global slowdown. Events in Argentina and in
international capital markets led to depreciation and required a tightening of monetary policy to
contain the inflationary pressure resulting from the depreciation. Fiscal policies were tightened
to maintain public debt sustainability in the face of higher interest rates and a depreciated
currency.  Finally, a domestic energy crisis with electricity rationing further constrained
economic activity. The domestic slowdown resulting from all these factors together means that
GDP growth in 2001 was only 1.6 percent. As the result of the negative external and internal
shocks, sovereign bond spreads rose, and the real depreciated by almost 40 percent in the first
nine months of 2001. However, it recovered by 16 percent in the last quarter of 2001, so that
depreciation over the year as a whole was only 16 percent. Over 2002, rates have been broadly
steady, inclined towards mild appreciation of 0.2 percent in the first quarter.
35.   As the result of effective macroeconomic management and a swift policy response,
Brazil is expected to withstand the current turbulences with reduced growth but without
major disruptions. The Government has maintained effective macroeconomic management and
reacted to the shocks with an appropriate mix of monetary and fiscal tightening and continued
push for structural reforms, together with successful energy rationing.13 Fiscal policy has
remained robustly credible, with the primary surplus to end-December, at R$43.7 billion,
exceeding the year-end target by R$3.5 billion. The year end primary fiscal surplus was 3.7
percent of GDP. The signing of a new IMF 15-month Standby Arrangement on September 14
lent further credibility at a difficult time. And finally, the continued assured management of
challenging macroeconomic circumstances by authorities has been a key signal to the markets.
Reflecting regained market confidence and underlining the recent "decoupling" effect, sovereign
spreads have fallen, and the real has strengthened from a yearly low of 2.78 to the dollar in mid-
October back to 2.40 in early December. The outlook for 2002 includes a GDP growth rate of
2.5 percent (against 1.6 percent achieved in 2001), a steady inflow of foreign direct investment,
and a reduced current account deficit.
36.   Brazil's public debt and external financing requirements imply continuing
vulnerability to future shocks, but this vulnerability is expected to decline gradually over
time. As a result of the depreciation and the increase in interest rates, the ratio of net public debt
to GDP rose to 54.8 percent in October 2001, compared with 48.6 percent in October 2000.
Public debt to GDP declined somewhat by end 2001, to 53.5 percent. The year-end decline
13 Which has successfully been phased out following the resumption of rains and the regeneration of hydropower.
Page 12



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
reinforces the conclusion that earlier increases during the year resulted almost entirely from the
depreciation of the exchange rate, rather than loose fiscal policy. Of the R$124 billion increase in
net debt over the past twelve months, 76.2 percent was attributed to the weakening currency.
Moreover an estimated fifth of the increased public sector borrowing requirement in late 2001
was due to the acknowledgement of public debts known as 'skeletons', such as the public bank
debt write offs as well as worthless or illiquid securities in state-run pension funds. Such
measures reduced hidden fiscal liabilities and made the govemment's accounts more transparent.
37.   In response, and consistent with the credible fiscal stance adopted over the last three
years, the Government raised its primary surplus target to 3.5 percent of GDP, which
ensures a declining debt to GDP ratio under the worsened circumstances. Regarding the
external accounts, the trade balance has improved through a compression of imports in response
to the depreciation. A surplus in the trade balance is expected for 2002. FDI has declined from
its record levels in the past two years but remains substantial even after the events of September
11, estimated to reach about US$19 billion for 2001. Current projections include a gradual
improvement in the current account balance (which was US$23.2 billion in 2001, or 4.5 percent
of GDP) and in external debt indicators.
38.   Brazil has continued the structural reforms necessary to reduce the vulnerabilities
related to its large public debt and high external rmancing requirements and needs to
further pursue these on a continuing basis. Improvements in the institutional framework for
fiscal management continue with the effective implementation of the Fiscal Responsibility Law
and refinements of the Plano Plurianual (PPA). Further public sector pension reform remains a
key item on the agenda of structural fiscal reforms. Financial sector reforms are proceeding,
including a first phase of federal bank reform. The recent signing of the new Corporate Law is
expected to promote development of Brazil's capital market through improved corporate
governance. With respect to other growth-promoting reforms, measures are being taken to
address the regulatory weaknesses that have led to the current energy crisis. Congress has
recently approved important measures to flexibilize the labor code. Other important medium-
term reforms should include improvements in the efficiency of the tax system.
39.   At this time, we foresee adjustment lending for fiscal reforms, fmancial sector
reforms, and energy sector reforms within the CAS lending ceilings. Adjustment lending
would support effective and continuing structural reforms to address the key objectives of fiscal
stability and renewed growth and would contribute to Brazil's external financing requirements of
US$47 billion and the nominal public sector deficit of 3.4 percent of GDP in 2002. The external
financing requirement consists of a US$20 billion current account deficit and US$27 billion
amortization of medium and long term debt. Financing is planned to be met by US$19 billion of
foreign direct investment and at least US$5 billion public sector borrowing including multilateral
disbursements, disbursements from privatization and interest revenues and the remainder by
other private sector rollover financing. In line with the CAS ceilings, the Bank is presenting an
Energy Sector Adjustment Loan and a Second Programmatic Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
in FY02, possibly followed by a Second Programmatic Fiscal Adjustment Loan in FY03.
40.   Unfavorable macroeconomic conditions drove inflation above its target band upper
limit of 6 percent. Inflation during 2001 was 7.67 percent, well above the 6 percent upper
ceiling, although there were improvements in the last quarter due to the containment of the
Page 13



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
energy crisis, a better than expected grain crop, and expected turnaround in the international
economy in 2002. Forecast inflation has been increased to 4.4 percent for 2002, an increase from
the level of 3.5 percent forecast a year ago. Baseline forecasts for 2003 have also increased to 2.8
percent, from 2.5 percent a year ago.
41.    Interest rates, which had exhibited a downward trend until early 2001, rose over
the year, as a response to deteriorating macroeconomic conditions including the depreciation
of the real in response to Argentine contagion and weaker international market sentiment after
September 11. The interbank SELIC rate was successively raised from 15.25 percent in January
2001 to 19 percent by July 2001. On September 21, the Central Bank was obliged to announce a
precautionary increase in reserve requirements on time deposits, of ten percent. 4 Reserve
requirements on demand deposits remain nominally unchanged at 45 percent, but their mode of
application grew stricter, increasing their impact on liquidity. Formerly, banks could deposit 60
percent of the amount due and deposit the remaining 40 percent in the following day. Now,
banks must deposit 80 percent of the required amount up front, and deposit 20 percent on the
following day. Additionally, the required shareholders equity formula for banks was modified to
increase the weight given to foreign exchange operations, thereby making it more costly for
banks to maintain any mismatch between foreign currency assets and liabilities. Interest rates are
high: real interbank rates are above 11 percent. 15 The base interest rate decline to adequate levels
is expected to occur only gradually over the medium term, particularly as improvements in fiscal
performance decrease the high levels of public sector indebtedness.
42.    But increases in bank spreads over 2001 are already being reversed with
improvements in macroeconomic conditions over early 2002.16 Signals of declining interest
rates in 2002 were made in February and March 2002 with successive small announcements of
declines in the interbank rate, by 25 basis points each, bringing the interbank rate down to 18.5
percent by end-March 2002. However, further cuts may be limited by oil price increases.
43.    Despite interest rate increases in 2001, credit growth continued, albeit at a slower
pace. Total bank credit, which had expanded at an annualized rate of about 20 percent in the last
months of 2000, slowed its growth to an average of below 10 percent in the twelve month period
ending October 2001, and further to 2.2 percent in the twelve months to February 2002. Overall
credit growth in 2001 was 4.2 percent. Retail credit expansion, which had surged to 70 percent a
year ago, slowed down to a twelve month average of 46.5 percent by end October 2001, and was
only 5.9 percent in the last three months of this period. Corporate lending was more resistant to
change, with a twelve month average increase of 34.5 percent, lower than the retail rate, but an
increase of 7.9 percent for the three months ending October; higher than the retail credit growth
rate.
14 Circular 3.062 of the Central Bank, of September 21, 2001. Reserve requirements on time deposits had been 30
percent at the start of 2000, and had been cut to zero only a year ago.
15 Average lending rates of financial institutions reached 65.8 percent per year in October 2001, increasing by 11
percentage points in the past twelve months. The average spread in October 2001 was 42.7 percent, declining to 39.9
percent in December but rising again to 41.8 percent in February 2002.

'conjunctural' or temporary factors, and underlying or 'persistent' factors.

Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
IV. BRAzIL's FINANCIAL SYSTEM - RECENT EVOLUTION AND SELECTED ISSUES
44.    A discussion of the broad structural aspects of a substantial spectrum of Brazil's financial
system has been provided in the documentation for the first programmatic adjustment loan.'7 The
present memorandum therefore focuses primarily on describing evolution over the past one year
and evaluating recent progress, in those areas of the financial system included in the proposed
operation. Selective depth and detailed treatment is given to specific areas of the financial system
which were not included or given less prominence in the first loan; in particular, the public banks
(where achieved and proposed reforms are discussed in Annex 2), and measures pertaining to
overall access to financial services (Annex 3).
45.   As the sections below show, overall progress with regard to the actions envisaged as part
of the programmatic financial reforms is substantial and in some areas actions have been taken in
program areas which exceed expectations and further deepen reform achievements. However, it
must be remembered that notwithstanding important actions taken, there is in many cases still a
long way to go. It is expected that a more detailed diagnostic of risks and vulnerabilities will be
revealed by the FSAP program, paving the way for incorporation into a third stage of
programmatic reform.
OVERALL FINANCIAL INTERMEDIATION
46.    A number of factors potentially impact upon the depth of overall financial intermediation,
identified to be shallow in Brazil in absolute as well as relative terms. Specific factors identified
in the first PFSECAL to have relatively greater significance in the context of Brazil included
high real interest rates and high spreads, high rates of taxation, costly systems for the
enforcement of credit contracts, the limited availability of adequate and reliable credit reporting
and debtor information, and a poor legal framework for the resolution of both enterprise
bankruptcy and bank failure. The sections below discuss progress over the past year in these
areas.
Interest Rates and Spreads
47.   Brazil's real interest rates are acknowledged by its authorities to be high, and are the
subject of constant and ongoing monitoring and analysis by its Central Bank, which has an
ongoing major research project under a large working group on interest rate movements, both
levels and spreads.18 High real lending rates have been attributed to a combination of factors,
some largely exogenous to financial institutions, such as the 'crowding out' effect of Brazil's very
high government borrowing requirements, high indirect taxation, forced investments and high
directed credit ratios, unremunerated reserve requirements and overhead costs thrice as high as in
OECD countries; as well as endogenous reasons such as loan-loss provisions, and inefficiency-
7 Report No. P7448-BR; April 26,2001.
18 The research group in the Central Bank in Sao Paulo which studies the issue of interest rate spreads has now
brought out two successive annual reports: 'Juros e Spread Bancdrio no Brasil'- November 2000 and November
2001, at http://www.bc.gov.br/ftp/jurospreadl 12001.pdf
Page 15



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
and cost-based causes. High overheads in turn have been attributed to limited competitive
pressures and the high costs of contract enforcement and debtor monitoring.
48.   Until early 2001, the favorable macroeconomic environment of lower interest rates and
non-inflationary growth contributed to the lowering of both rates and spreads. 19 As of December
2000, average nominal lending rates were around 51 percent (down from 62.3 percent at the end
of 1999) while average spreads were about 35 percentage points (also down nine percent from
the previous year), with spreads for corporate lending at 19 percentage points and for consumer
lending at 51 percentage points.20 Towards the end of 2001, average lending rates for financial
institutions reached 66 percent, increasing by 11 percent over the last twelve months. Average
spreads showed a similar increase rising to almost 47 percent. The recent increase in the reserve
requirements on time deposits from zero to ten percent is a reversal from the reduction last year
of unremunerated reserve requirements on sight deposits from 75 to 45 percent and on time
deposits to zero. However, these were measures the government was obliged to take, in an effort
to tighten monetary policy in response to the potentially inflationary impact of this year's
currency depreciation. Although efforts to decrease interest rates and spreads have clearly
been less successful over the last months this can be ascribed largely to macroeconomic
volatiity and uncertainty triggered by external events. Moreover, recent rate cuts in February
and March 2002 signal the government's intention to reduce rates as rapidly as is consistent with
macroeconomic policy. Analysis by the Central Bank of recent increases in the short-term bank
spread show that the upward trend of 2001 is largely due to temporary factors and that
underlying trend behavior is not affected.21 A second paper exploring the bank-level variance of
ex-ante interest rate spreads in Brazil has underlined the importance of the macroeconomic
environment as well as operating costs, as determinants of interest rate spreads.22 Despite these
efforts it must be acknowledged that in absolute terms, spreads in Brazil remain very high and
that a reduction in spreads must remain prominent on the agenda for future reform.
49.   Authorities are making efforts to foster competition in interest rates through enhanced
disclosure. When the project on interest rates and banking spreads was released, the Central
Bank started to disclose basic information on interest rates charged by financial institutions on its
website. It also introduced requirements to increase the quality and the degree of detail of this
information.
50.   A proportion of credit in Brazil is still directed by the government, in the sense that
interest rates are controlled, at positive but below market rates, and also that there are selective
credit policies which earmark credit for specific sectors of the economy, leading to distortions of
interest rates. Since June 2000, however, efforts have been made to track the proportion of
directed credit, and there has been a significant decline in its proportion over the last two
years, from close to 55 percent in June 2000 to around 40 percent by end-2001. The federal
19 Garcfa-Cantera et al. (2001) attributed much of the reductions of the 2000 lending spread to the reduction in the
level and volatility of interest rates.
20 Boletim do Banco Central do Brasil - Annual Report. Various Issues
21 Banco Central, FOCUS report of December 11, 2001.
22 Afanasieff, Villa Lhacer and Nakane (2001): 'The Determinants of Bank Interest Spread in Brazil', Banco
Central do Brasil, nimeo. The paper also shows that foreign banks have lower interest spreads than domestic banks.

banks have been primary vehicles for directed credit policies and ongoing efforts at federal bank
reform are likely to help to further reduce this proportion.23 Important initiatives are now under
discussion in the context of reform of credit directed towards housing via savings deposits. A
modification to Law 4591 on the Sistema Financeira de Habita,ao is under preparation and is
expected to be ready in the next few months, which will eliminate the tax advantage given to
savings deposits, but instead remunerate them at market rates, thus reducing financial distortions
and also lifting the requirement that these deposits be used for housing finance. Savings from
the elimination of the tax break will instead be used to pay directly for low income housing.
Taxation
51.    Despite macroeconomic turbulence, the Central Bank has continued to try to reduce
overhead costs (through improving contract enforcement, better credit / debtor information, and
measures to foster competition) and through efforts to formulate a rationalization of tax policy
towards the financial sector. The Brazilian tax system exhibits several distortionary taxes on both
financial institutions and transactions. Two social security taxes (COFINS and PIS) are
calculated on the basis of total revenues, adjusted for funding costs, rather than profits. The
CPMF is collected on virtually all financial transactions, but there are exemptions for some types
of institutions. Further, the IOF is collected on loans and short-term foreign exchange
operations.24 Although of less significance than the CPMF, its discretionary character, with rates
set by the Central Bank, leaves the door open for distortionary effects.
