Annex 2 - Public Bank Reforms
ANNEx 2: BRAzIL's PUBLIC BANK REFORMS
1.    The recent acceleration of Brazil's program of public bank reform may be viewed as a new
and major step forward in a process which had its genesis in a program of bank strengthening
which began in 1995, shortly after the launching of the Real plan, with the PROER and PROES
programs for the restructuring of private and state government owned financial institutions,
aimed at preventing financial crises, enhancing financial system soundness and integrity as well
as protecting against fiscal indiscipline at the state level.' The new step forwards in reform,
launched in June 2001, for the first time address problems at the country's largest, federally-
owned financial institutions. These initiatives are backed by new prudential regulations and
greatly strengthened banking supervision which requires compliance with regulatory norms at
the federal banks, and also requires the government as owner to take cognizance and action to
ensure the solvency of federal financial institutions. Recent actions however may be viewed as
the first phase of a further series of measures to ensure sustainability of the reforms. The present
note first discusses the principles and challenges of the recent federal bank reforms, then details
specific steps taken at individual federal financial institutions for their financial restructuring,
and in the final section, provides an overview of progress with the rationalization and
privatization of state-owned financial institutions.
I. STRENGTHENING FEDERAL BANKS - OVERVIEW OF RECENT ACTIONS
2.    Brazil has four deposit taking federally owned financial institutions; Banco do Brasil
(BdB) and the Caixa Econ6mica Federal (CEF), which are the two largest deposit banks in the
country, and two smaller regional federal banks, the Banco do Nordeste do Brasil (BNB), and
Banco da Amaz6nia - BASA. Over the years, these banks, which have been used to implement
specially designed and targeted programs for lending to specific sections of the economy
(notably rural and housing finance programs and regional development programs), have suffered
continued deterioration of their loan portfolio, as a result of a combination of factors; their
IThe Program of Incentives for the Restructuring and Strengthening of the National Financial System (PROER),
which mandated and laid down rules for bank mergers and incorporations, was launched by the issue of Medida
Provisoria No. 1179 and Resolution No. 2208, both issued on March 11, 1995. Following closely upon the crisis
involving Banco Economico (the 22nd bank subjected to intervention/liquidation since implementation of the Real
Plan on July 1, 1994) it was viewed by the government as a means of early intervention and crisis prevention,
facilitating the process of financial system adjustment. Virtually simultaneous were the adoption of the principle of
depositor protection, (National Monetary Council Resolutions no. 2197, dated August 31, 1995, and 2211, dated
November 16, 1995), and the issue of Provisional Measure No. 1182, on November 17, 1995, which gave the
Central Bank the legal instruments that it needed for intervention. A year later, the PROES program, for
restructuring state banks, was launched by the issue of Medida Provisoria No 1514, on August 7, 1996, which
established mechanisms designed to promote reduction of the involvement of the state public sector in financial and
banking activities, providing for the privatization of financial institutions, and greatly expanding the preventive
powers of the Central Bank.
Annex 2 Page 1 of 17



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
special mandates and programs, limited supervision and inspection, differential norms for the
roles of managers and keeping of accounts for federal banks relative to private banks, and also,
the problems of internal efficiency and unclear accountability.
3.   Following the issue of a series of new prudential regulations for the banking system from
1999, and the major improvements introduced in its systems of on-site bank examination and
supervision, the Central Bank of Brazil launched a comprehensive program of on-site
examinations ("Global Consolidated Inspections", or GCI) in the first half of the year 2000, for
the federal banks. Through this massive and unprecedented effort, the Central Bank intended to
identify all the required adjustments to the balance sheets of the four federal banks in order to
require them to comply with key prudential regulations issued during the last few years,
including Resolution 2099/94 on capital adeqacy and recent modifications to this Resolution
which significantly increase the risk weight applied to tax credits;2 Resolutions 2682/99, that
establishes new rules to classify and provision problem loans; Resolution 2692/00, that includes
market risk as part of the capital requirements; and Resolution 2827/01, that extends and imposes
new maximum lending limits for credits granted to the public sector. The inspections explicitly
revealed problems that have existed in the four banks for years; a significant proportion of which
were due to credit operations that the federal banks make on behalf of the federal government.
4. Negotiations between the government, as owner of the Federal Banks, and the Central
Bank, as regulator and supervisor, resulted in a series of decisions to restructure and recapitalize
the federal banks, and going forward, to introduce transparency in their operations with the
government. The outcome of decisions made, and the program launched for the strengthening of
these federal banks was reflected in a Decree Law, ("Medida Provis6ria ") No. 2196-1, issued on
June 22, 2001. The main objectives spelled out in this law were to require the owner (the Federal
Government) to restore the financial condition of these banks, recognizing and absorbing past
losses. Going forward the Federal Government undertakes to assume the risks and costs of the
various federal credit programs managed by these institutions, reflecting transparently in its
budget any subsidies provided through these banks, which henceforth must be approved by the
National Congress. The Decree Law of June 2001 provides specifically for:
*  Removing the credit risk from the operations funded by the so called "Constitutional
Funds" managed by these banks prior to November 30, 1998;3
*  Allowing these banks to manage new "Constitutional Funds" at their own risk. The banks
can decide whether or not they wish to share the credit risk of such loans, receiving
higher returns if they agree to bear such risks;4
2 To 300 percent.
3There are three regional Constitutional funds, managed by three of the four FFI; the North-East fund , (managed
by BNB), the Northern fund (managed by BASA), and the Center-East fund (managed by BdB). These funds have
been used as vehicles for the implementation of social policy objectives through credit allocation, channeling funds
to certain activities designated by the government.
Annex 2 Page 2 of 17



Annex 2 - Public Bank Reforms
*  Authorizing the Government to void guarantees given by the federal banks for rural
credits sold to the Federal Government at book value. Thus these banks would not bear
any further responsibility for credit risk on the rural and real estate loans swapped with
the Federation for new, negotiable, debt;5
*  Authorizing the Federation to net out the debit and credit accounts of the federal banks
with the Federation;
*  Establishing special debt-for-bond swap programs for various programs/debts held by
each of the four federal banks.
5.   The recapitalization actions undertaken to execute the "Medida Provis6ria" are intended to
constitute the first stage of a transformation process which aims to have the federal banks
governed more independently, with their senior managers accountable to commercially oriented
performance targets, which are better specified and communicated internally, and determined in
advance within the framework of each institutions' strategic plan. The overall transformation
process therefore also envisages internal changes which will constitute a second phase of
reorganization, intended to improve corporate govemance, diversify business activities away
from government programs in order to strengthen profitability and improve transparency and
accountability, and strengthen and render transparent internal information and controls, and in
order to continue and sustain the recapitalization measures executed last June. The federal banks
are already reported to be immersed in their strategic planning process for the 2002-04 triennial.
Specifically, the internal changes, actively supported and closely monitored by the Central Bank,
are envisaged to include:
*  Leveraging   management layers by     creating  a structure  of accountability  with
decentralized reporting, providing for more objective and commercial decision making;
*  Developing a new commercial strategy and new products to diversify away from their
traditional targeted sectors and Government-oriented programs;
*  Organizing activities by major business lines and separating out Government-related
activities;
*  Improving management information systems to support the monitoring of the above
actions and to view the performance of each major business line, especially for
Govemment programs, hence enabling performance monitoring on a risk adjusted basis;
*  Improving controls according to plans, and within a timetable required by the Central
Bank.
6.    The overall fiscal cost of the program in stock terms of new debt issues, as estimated by
the government, is described in the following table. Of the gross securities on issue for each
4Under option (i) the bank would assume a hundred percent of the credit risk and receive a six percent return from

(subordinated debt) subject to the overall limit that Tier 2 capital cannot exceed fifty percent of the banks' Tier 1
capital. Under option (ii) the bank only assumes fifty percent of the credit risk, receives a lower return (three
percent), and cannot deem funds received to be part of its Tier 2 capital.

no immediate impact on the public sector net debt. However, new bond issues for each
institution, as described in the last column, will increase net public sector debt by R$12.5 billion
or around one percent of GDP. The restructuring will have no impact on the central government
primary surplus, but the increase in public debt will increase public interest expenditures. If
assets transferred from the banks (R$29.5 billion or around 2.5 percent of GDP) are not fully
recovered, there could also be future fiscal costs to the extent of non-recovery.

ACHEVEMENTS AND CHALLENGES OF THE FEDERAL BANK REFORM PROGRAM
7.   Clearly, these actions may be viewed as significant and major first steps towards the
reform of the largest financial institutions in Brazil. Based on the underlying new prudential
requirements, continued enforcement of compliance of these regulations will help to ensure that
financial soundness is sustained. The decision to inject new government securities in the
institutions which are liquid and remunerated at market rates is also a significant advance over
early attempts at financial bailouts over a decade ago, provided the banks are indeed permitted
free use of these resources. Decisions to increase the transparency of government mandates are
laudable. The adoption of a two-phase program (and potentially a multi-pase program) to
simultaneously reform internal controls, audit, management information and governance systems
will be the key to reform sustainability.
8.   Going forward, several challenges still remain ahead to ensure the sustainability of
reforms. This will require close cooperation and a shared vision between the government as the
owner, the Central Bank as the banking supervisor, and senior management at the banks. First,
the government as owner needs to ensure that directors serving on the boards of the federal
banks are suitably qualified professionals, with the independence to support and oversee the
execution of the internal changes that are being initiated. Today, standard fit-and-proper criteria
for private bank directors are not applicable to public banks. Public bank directors may be
politically appointed and are thus vulerable to political processes. New strategic plans and
accompanying programs for action must therefore be adopted as binding goals, so that they
better endure changes after the forthcoming elections. Regulatory requirements on governance,
fitness and enforcement should be achieved by incorporation into the by-laws of public banks,
including the details on the profile, qualifications and role of directors serving in the board, and
Annex 2 Page 4 of 17



Annex 2 - Public Bank Reforms
the composition and functions of core committees of the board.6 In this context, the recently
announced decision of the government to pernit the increase in public shareholding of Banco do
Brasil, from the present level of 8.7 percent to 25 percent, to permit its eventual listing on the
Novo Mercado, which indicates adherence to certain principles of good governance, is notable.
9.   Second, senior management of the federal financial institutions needs to have a business
strategy that optimizes the potential of their banking franchise, including the real capital to
finance it, and the systems for the Boards to control, and the Central Bank to monitor, its
execution. Senior management teams have to build reasonable future plans on the
recapitalization programs executed, so that they can preserve the necessary financial
sustainability without incurring new unwarranted risks for which they are not yet prepared
(derived from suddenly diversifying to new market segments). This should include measures to
address the poor efficiency of both banks and rationalize their costs structures without relying
solely on gains from business volumes. The strategies should consider a solution to the
duplication of distribution channels between BdB and CEF, and the sustainability of their
monopolistic access to cheap funding with regard to private sector banks. To the extent that
federal banks are still expected by government to undertake developmental functions,
through the management of special funds or other means, this should be based on the
banks'own risk assessments and remuneration should be at market rates.
10. Third, the Central Bank has to augment the scope of its supervisory approach to
enforce best standards (of fit and properness, corporate risk governance, and control and
information systems) and also to add qualitative judgment in evaluating and monitoring the new
strategy and accompanying financial and capital plans of the federal financial institutions. The
Central Bank would need to achieve this without diminishing the surveillance and examination
efforts to assess their continuous solvency. Once the Central Bank communicates those standards
and finalizes its procedures and methodology to assess adherence to them, it would gain more
capacity to hold the boards of the federal banks accountable to both current quantitative
prudential regulations and to sounder corporate governance and risk management practices. This
can provide an additional layer of protection to extemal policy changes and mitigate any major
deviation from the newly defined commercial goals of the FFI.
11. Recognizing this need, the Central Bank has begun to tighten further its supervision of the
federal financial institutions. In early 2002, a second round of on-site inspections was
launched in the four major banks, focusing on specific areas: management information systems,
the internal audit function and process, credit risk management and models used internally, the
overall risk management function and its processes, and additionally, a partial credit review.
12. Finally, the continued substantial management of special funds by the federal banks
remains a source of potential vulnerability in the future. Three of the four federal banks manage
regional development Constitutionl funds: the North-East fund - FNE, (mananged by BNB), the
Northern fund - FNO (managed by BASA), and the Center-East fund (managed by BdB) - FCO.
In addition, each bank manages additional special funds. In the case of BdB, it is an important
6The Central bank is trying to introduce such changes, in particular to incorporate improvements in fit-and-proper
criteria to serve on Boards of Directors, and introduce greater independence in the functioning of the internal 'fiscal
Board' or inspection committee; in addition to proposal for changes in MIS and internal controls.

Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
agent for the Federal Government's Defense Fund for the Coffee Economy (FUNCAFE),
PRONAF (National Program for the Strengthening of Family-based Agriculture) and
PROCEDER II, the Development Program of the Cerrado Area. Caixa Economica Federal,
although not a recipient of regional funds, nevertheless also had trusteeship responsibilities for
special programs eg through the FGTS, used for housing schemes for the disadvantaged. BNE
has a trusteeship for the FNE fund, the scale of which dwarfs BNO's own operations, and also for
the FINOR, the Northeast Development Fund. Finally, BASA administers, in addition to the
FNO fund, significant volumes of funds from prgrams such as PRONAF and PRORURAL
(Rural Family-Organized Agricultural Production Support Program). Alternative mechanisms for
achieving the embedded social objectives of these programs, without impacting on the financial
soundness of the banks, will need to be assured.7
II. MEASURES TAKEN TO STRENGTHEN INDIVIDUAL BANKS
BANCO DO BRASIL
13.  Banco do Brasil (BdB) is the holding of a large conglomerate of about 23 financial sector
companies. BdB is the largest universal bank in Brazil, with total consolidated assets, as of June
30, 2001, of R$146.7 billion.8 At the end of June 2001, BdB maintained the first position in
market share among the seventeen largest Brazilian banks for total assets (20.7 percent); deposits
(25.2 percent); and, loans (22.1 percent). However, in terms of returns to assets, according to
market analyses, it ranks 16t with 0.43 percent, while for returns on equity it ranks 15'h, with
7.53 percent, among the seventeen largest banks in the country. Its ratios of overheads rank 13'h,
with 75.66 percent, and equity to assets ranks 14'h, with 5.58 percent. Banco do Brasil has a
tremendous franchise value and potential for selling financial products based on its extensive
network (with the largest branch network, of 3,091 branches and a 22.7 percent share in branch
offices).
14. The government maintains a 71.8 percent participation in BdB held by the Treasury. BdB's
Employees Pension Fund (Previ) is its second largest shareholder with a 13.8 percent
participation. Banco do Brasil has a large number of personnel (89,568 employees, second only
to CEF among the top 17), as manifested by its headcount ratio of 29 employees per branch (in
contrast to 23 for the largest fifteen banks excluding BdB and CEF). BdB's stand-alone financial
strength (Moody's) and individual strength (IBCA) ratings rank at the levels of E and D/E,
respectively, i.e., in the lower segment, without considering its potential recourse to, or support
by, the Brazilian State. The principal measures taken for the restructuring of Banco do Brasil are
summarized below.
Restructuring Undertaken - External Debt: 'Brady'Bond Swap
15. Since 1993 BdB had held a portfolio of Brady bonds which had grown increasingly
illiquid. Under the restructuring, the Treasury swapped Bradys for domestic Treasury
7The role and operation of the various special programs operated by the Federal banks will be explored in greater
detail in a forthcoming Bank study on "Access to Financial services in Brazil" (World Bank, 2002).
8Source: Salomon Smith Barney, Brazilian Bank Reference Guides; June 2001 and October 2001.
Annex 2 Page 6 of 17



Annex 2 - Public Bank Reforms
bonds, at market value. The Brady bonds were retired on June 29, 2001. This measure relieved
BdB from the need to provision for the marking-to-market of the Brady bonds, thus improving
profitability, improving liquidity and reducing exposure to exchange rate variations
Restructuring - Securitzation of Rural Debts Under Law 9138/95 - Own Resources and Third-
Party Resources
16. BdB also held a package of securitized agricultural loans. For a part of these, BdB's joint
liability with the State was eliminated marking down BdB's liability to zero percent in place of
50 percent, leading to a reduction in BdB's risk-weighted assets. In addition, BdB will no
longer be subject to credit risk from these operations, thus eliminating the need for provisions
against these assets. New credit risk under these programs, not evaluated by BdB's credit
scoring system will be assumed by the National Treasury, and BdB's credit risk exposure will
therefore be reduced, although with a correspondingly smaller spread.
17.  Another part of the securitized agricultural loans held by BdB were on account of deposits
made by third parties. BdB has been exonerated from the credit risk of these operations
contracted with resources from the Federal Government Development Bank (BNDES), the
Federal Government's Fund for the Development of the Central-Western Region of Brazil
(FCO), the Federal Government's Defense Fund for the Coffee Economy (FUNCAFt) and other
National Treasury funds, thus further decreasing BdB's risk-weighted assets as well as its need
for provisioning for these loans.
Federal Constitutional Fund (FCO) for the Development of the Center-West
18. The Treasury assumed the risk of FCO credits contracted up to November 30, 1998, and
BdB assumed shared risk in operations after that date. This measure should also reduce its risk-
weighted assets. BdB would also lose the interest income of all loans funded with FCO granted
until November 1998, since the credit risk of these operations has passed to FCO itself. Also
from July 2001, in view of their long term nature, the Central Bank allowed BdB to count
resources held in the FCO Constitutional fund as Tier II capital. BdB thus became the first bank
in Brazil to have Tier II capital. BdB thus stopped receiving the management fee from FCO,
equivalent to three percent of the FCO's net worth.
FUNCAFI and PRODECER II (Development Program of the Cerrado Area)
19. Credits contracted with FUNCAFt and PRODECER II funds were also forgiven, leading
to a further reduction in risk-weighted assets.
PESA (Special Program for Asset Recovery)
20. For loans to rural borrowers contracted under the PESA program before 1999, the Treasury
has agreed to take over these loans at their face value in exchange for federal public bonds
(LFTs). The transfer of this portfolio provides a reduction in risk-weighted assets and also a
reduction in provisioning as BdB will no longer be subject to credit risk from these operations.
Accordingly, this measure implies a swap of non-performing assets which yield practically zero
for public securities remunerated at market (Selic) linked rates.

Summary of Measures and Restructuring Transactions
21.  The measures adopted allow BdB to (i) reduce its risk-weighted assets by R$6.8 billion
and its required equity by R$751 million; (ii) increase its equity value in the amount of R$3
billion and thus to adjust its capital to minimum standards. BdB's new capital adequacy ratio will
therefore be around 12 percent, versus 8 percent as of April 30.
CAIXA ECONOMIUCA FE-DERAL
22. Caixa Econgmica Federal (CEF) is the second largest federal financial institution operating
in Brazil. Caixa is fully controlled by the Federal goverment and had, as of June 30, 2001,
consolidated assets of R$95.8 billion,9 down about 24 percent from R$125.7 billion in December
2000, due to the effects of the strengthening program. At the end of 2000, Caixa ranked first in
market share for total loans among the largest seventeen Brazilan banks (24.7 percent); the
second for deposits (24.2 percent) and total assets (19.4 percent). Since June 2001, it has ranked
second for deposits (23.4 percent), and fourth for loans (10.5 percent). Caixa also had the lowest
profitability among Brazil's large banks, with negative retumns on assets (-7.9 percent) and
returns on equity (-242 percent) as of June 2001.10 As of December 2001, Caixa ranked 15'h in
returns on assets (0.21 percent) and 1 1th in returns on equity (7.7 percent). Its ratios of overheads
also stand at the end of the ranking amnong the first seventeen largest banks in the Brazil. Caixa
has a valuable franchise and potential for cross selling financial products based on its extensive
network (1,643 branches, 3d with an 11.7 percent share). But it also appears to have high staff
ratios as manifested by its headcount ratio of 62 employees per branch (compared to an average
of 23 for the largest fifteen banks excluding BdB and CEF).
9Source: Salomon Smith Barney, Brazilian Bank Reference Guide June 2001, October 9, 2001.
10 These large figures for end-June partly reflect the adjustments explained in the text, to conform to new prudential
regulations.

Restructuring Program of Caixa
23. Principal actions include: (i) transfer of problem real estate loans to a special purpose non-
financial company EMGEA; (ii) assumptions by the govemment of Caixa's debts due to special
Funds it manages for the government; (iii) sale of poor infrastructure loans to the government in
exchange for Treasury Bills; (iv) an increase of CEF's capital by converting debts previously
acquired by Caixa under the PROER program; (v) a swap of CVS securities for new govemment
bonds (LFTs); (vi) the future swap of CVS bonds for LFTs at the moment of renewing FCVS
loans; and (vii) the advanced settlement of debts with the FGTS by means of payment with CVS
bonds.
Transfer of real estate loans to EMGEA
24. A large part of Caixa's total loan portfolio of R$34.7 billion in April 2001, consisted of
'skeletons'; bad real estate loans contracted before 1995. These were transferred to a special
purpose non-financial corporation, EMGEA, created by the government. Loans transferred
include around R$15 billion which were already a liability to the Treasury and were covered by
FCVS bonds (Fundo de CompensaVdo das Variaqdes Salariais); some were without coverage."I
In payment, the government assumed a large proportion of Caixa's debts to FGTS (the Federal
Unemployment Fund), FAHBRE and FDS. These measures reduce the need for provisioning
and needs for additional capital. The government will exchange new CVS bonds for the bad
assets that were to be covered by FCVS bonds.
Early settlement of Caixa's debts with FGTS
25. This measure entails the cancellation of Caixa's debts with FGTS using CVS bonds as
mean of payment, further relieving Caixa of provisioning needs.
Transfer to the government of the credit risk in loans funded with FGTS resources
26. The government also acquired the remainder of Caixa's loan portfolio, consisting largely
of urban and infrastructure loans granted with the resources of FGTS, and paying below market
interest, replacing them with newly issued market interest bearing Treasury Bills (Selic-indexed
LFTs). This measure reduces the need for provisioning and additional capital requirements.
Swap of problem loans with government bonds
27. Caixa's assets included loans made against assets received from the government in a
previous refinancing transaction, referred to as Law 8727/93 (debt restructuring from states and
municipalities). These loans were also exchanged for new Treasury bills (LFTs). Although this
11 The FCVS was a treasury fund created in the high inflation era to compensate banks for losses made in providing
cheap mortgage loans, which had inadequate inflation indexing. Although the bonds carry a sovereign risk, they
carry a below market coupon, benefit from a grace period for interest and principal repayments, are illiquid and
marked to market would be heavily discounted.

Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
measure does not have any direct impact on provisioning or capital requirements, since the debts
were guaranteed by the federal government, it will have a major effect in improving the liquidity
and profitability of Caixa.
Recapitalization - Assumption of CEFs debts (PROER) by the National Treasury
28. The Treasury also assumed Caixa's liabilities to the Central Bank (due to operations
carried in connection with PROER involving the acquisition of housing loans from problem
banks and converted .into capital. at CEF). It paid the Central Bank with Treasury bonds. This
measure reduces the liabilities of Caixa with the Central Bank raising its capital. Besides the
increase in capital, this measure will improve Caixa's operational cash flow and margins
substantially saving annual funding costs. A part of the new capital has been used to provision
against bad loans.
Summary of Measures and Restructuring Transactions
29. The measures adopted allow Caixa to reduce its estimated losses in the amount of R$52.7
billion and increase its equity by R$9.4 billion, conforming to minimum capital requirements.
CEF's new capital adequacy ratio is 14.8 percent. Caixa's balance sheet has shrunk by about a
third, to around R$90 billion.

BANCO Do NORDESTE Do BRASEL (BNB)
31. The bank was established under Law Number 1649 of July 1952 and began to operate in
1954 in Fortaleza. As a Government Development Bank, it's mission is to 'contribute to the
sustained development of the Northeast region of Brazil, to promote the region's economic
integration with Brazilian and international economies and to work towards reducing regional
and social inequality.' Many of the programs it finances constitute a part of social development
plans promoted by the federal government. BNB operates, among other incentive programs, the
FNE (Constitutional Fund of Development of the Northeast) and FINOR (Northeast
Development Fund) programs. The bank also operates as a commercial bank in the Northeast
and regards this function as complimentary to its development function. Its present strong
management team is competent, knowledgeable and attempts to operate the bank along the lines
of a commercial bank. The Directors of BNB, as with all Federal Banks are politically appointed
and can be replaced by the new Government which will be voted in November of 2002.
32. As a development bank, BNB's activities are long term in nature, and the bank's viability
depends to a significant extent on the availability of continuous FNE funds. A major portion of
the bank's income consists of fees received for the Administrated Funds. BNB's liabilities
consist largely of deposits from the BNDES bank, including its FINAME subsidiary and the
FAT (Workers Support Fund). Approximately half of the credit extended by BNB is channeled
in accordance with specific developmental criteria to the Agricultural and Industrial sectors.
33. The June 2001 Government Strengthening program announced its intention of addressing
BNB's needs for financial restructuring through the following measures:
* A capital injection in the form of govemment securities. Budgetary approval was sought
for R$2.6 billion for the year 2000 for this purpose, which was transferred on December
28, 2001.
* Exclusion of rural credit operations (Law No. 9138/95) from the calculations of risk
weighted assets of BNB. The Federal Government will assume the credit risk of those
operations.
* Exemption from the credit risk of FNE operations before November 1998.
* Use of FNE funds as Tier-2 capital if it assumes 100 percent of credit risk.
34. BNB has prepared a December-31, 2001 financial statement reflecting both adjustments to
loan losses as well as the new capital injection.
BANCO DA AMAZONIA (BASA)
35. BASA was incorporated in 1966 with the mission of promoting social and economic
development as a financing agent of the Federal Government for the Region of Amazonia. It
was with the advent of the 1988 Constitution that the Northern Region gained a stable source of
financing for productive activities, These funds enabled BASA to effectively intervene in the
Region. Over the last eleven years BASA has entered into approximately 140 thousand credit
operations in the rural sector, with 87 percent of these going to small scale family-based
production through the National Program for Strengthening Family based Agriculture
(PRONAF), and the Rural Family-Organized Agricultural Production Support Program
Annex 2 Page 11 of 17



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
(PRORURAL). BASA's branch network totals 82 branches and 15 banking locations. Outside
its own geographic area it has established branches in 4 major cities of the country in order to be
able to raise additional funds for its operations.
36. Over the period of three years, 1998 through 2000, the bank's profitability  has
experienced significant volatility, jumping from R$25 million in 1998 to R$74 million in 1999
and decreasing to R$51 million in 2000.
37. In the case of BASA, the Strengthening Program envisaged
*  A capital injection of about R$1.05 billion.
*  Exemption of credit risk from FNO operations granted before Nov/1998, which amounts
to R$1.4 billion.
* Elimination of BASA' s payment obligations of securitized operations
*  Use of FNE funds as tier-2 capital if it assumes 100 percent of credit risk.
* Exchange of long-term loans granted under Law No. 8727 of November 1993 for bonds
of the national treasury. This would allow BASA to cover the deficit of its pension fund
(Fundo de Pensdo dos Empregados - CAPAF).
38. BASA's end of the year financial statement for 2001 reflects these adjustments.
III. STATE BANK REFORM - PROGRAM BACKGROUND
39. In mid-1996 the Government of Brazil instituted the "Program of Incentives for the
Reduction of the States' Participation in Banking Activities" (PROES), aiming at curbing the
banking activities of subnational entities (States and Municipalities).12 The intention was both to
curb credit expansion to such entities, which had contributed to the fiscal and monetary disarray
which led to the hyperinflations suffered by Brazil in the 1980's and early 1990's, and also to
reduce possible contingent liabilities of the Federal Government and local governments. The
Cardoso Administration was also interested in increasing the presence of stronger private entities
to foster more competition and the exit of insolvent banking institutions which were not
complying with minimal prudential norms but were in nevertheless in operation and in
competition with compliant financial institutions.
40. Since March 1998, fiscal agreements between the States and the Federal Government
included agreements with the States to reduce their indebtedness. And even before this, earlier
agreements (1986) required States to comply with rules prohibiting or at least restricting direct
financing from their own banks.  But in practice, these rules were bypassed through
"triangulation" mechanisms through state suppliers or foreign branches of the state banks,
progressively raising the states' indebtedness with sub-nationally owned banks. Under the
PROES program and until 2010, state bond issues are forbidden and rollovers are limited to no
more than 95 percent of redemptions.
12As described in footnote 1 in this annex.
Annex 2 Page 12 of 17



Annex 2 - Public Bank Reforns
41.   Under PROES, sub-national govemments were presented with five options:
*   Restructuring the banks owned by them;
* Transferring the control of these banks to the Federal Government;
*   Selling these banks to the private sector;
*   Transforming these banks into development agencies; or
*  Closing these banks.
42. In exchange for any of these five actions, the National Treasury financed either fifty
percent or a hundred percent of the owner's liabilities; fifty percent if the institution remained in
operation as a commercial bank, or up to a hundred percent in other cases. Financing was offered
at 6 percent per annum, with an inflation index (IGP-DI) applied to the value of liabilities. In
exchange for the debt sub-national governments would receive two types of Government paper
(LFT-As and LFI'-Bs) with up to 15-year maturity and, in some cases, with monthly
amortization payments. An additional enhancement was the option to swap the LFT- for cash.
43. It should be pointed out that in parallel to pressures from the federal government to change
the role of state banks, state governments too became less anxious to retain their banks, as they
diminished in importance as revenue sources with the loss in float income. Additionally, with the
introduction of Central Bank regulations on loan classification and provisioning, ownership of
state banks threatened to become a liability. Thus many state governments began to participate
with new interest in the federal government's privatization proposals.
44. Private, local or foreign, commercial bank buyers were offered a number of incentives to
purchase these State banks. These included: (i) time-bound exemptions from the Central Bank's
reserve requirements on bank deposits (regulatory forbearance); (ii) the use of tax credits; (iii)
guarantees that public sector employees' accounts would be kept at the State level for five years;
and (iv) use of 'privatization currencies'."3 These 'sweetners' have played a significant role in
some privatizations. The use of privatization currencies at a discount of roughly 50 percent was
an accepted form of payment in the auctions of Banerj and CrediReal (state banks of Rio de
Janeiro and Minhas Gerais, respectively). More recently, 74 percent of the privatization of BEG,
the state bank of Goias, in December 2001, was paid for in government securities. The value of
other incentives such as tax credits and exemptions from reserve requirements has diminished
over time. Tax credits were more relevant in the period before 1999, when private banks had a
low volume of such credits and interest rates were high. 14 Exemptions from reserve requirements
13 Debts contracted in the past with the federal government netted out against payments for acquisition of state-
owned companies. Acceptable privatization currencies including select Treasury notes in the series NTN-M,
mortgage notes of the CEF, agricultural debt notes (TDA), and others. (Details are available in
httpJ/www.bndes.gov.br/english/coinsing.htm) The minimum cash percentage acceptable for each privatization is
determined by the President of the republic.
14 So that low cost deposits could profitably be invested in low-risk government securities, and tax credits were
quickly used. But today, private banks on average in Brazil have tax credits representing 30 percent of their equity,
and interest rates have halved; thus the time in which tax credits can be used is extended and their current value is
reduced
Annex 2 Page 13 of 17



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
have also been of reduced value with overall interest rate and reserve requirement reductions, as
in 2000.
45. State bank acquisitions in Brazil have been a part of a larger process of consolidation in the
banking system, where a number of small private banks have also been acquired."5 The three
largest private banks, Bradesco, Itaui and Unibanco, have been responsible for the lion's share of
acquisitions of state banks in the sector since 1997, when the state bank privatization began.
According to analysts, early bank acquisitions, including the state bank privatizations such as
Banerj and Bemge (both acquired by Itau), and Credireal, (which was bought by BCN/Bradesco)
were quite cheap. The consolidation process was just beginning, and high domestic interest rates
(with real rates above 25 percent) effectively overrode the need for scale, as a bank could
continue not lending while still registering comfortable margins. In the second phase, after the
devaluation of 1999 and the consequent loss of 'float' income, scale of operations, and the
value of a branch network, began to increase in relevance, with falling domestic interest rates,
and a rising shares of loans in bank assets. State bank acquisitions grew much more expensive, as
witnessed by the high premium paid in the acquisition of Banestado by Itau' (R$1.65 billion, and
a premium of 303 percent over the reserve price).'6
46. In the third and current phase, scale in the retail banking sector has become a key focus
foreign banks have realized the need to grow in scale to maintain a relevant role, and there is an
increasing scarcity of alternatives, for raising scale. Recent prices paid have exceeded
expectations. The present stage began with the privatization of Banespa in November 2000, and
intensified thereafter with the privatization of BEG, sold to Itau for R$665 million (US$271
million); more than twice its book value, a year later. Privatizations in 2001, which resumed
after a year long hiatus, have again accelerated in pace. Together with the sale of BEG, two
further sales concluded in late 2001 and early 2002; Banco do Estado da Paraiba (sold to ABN
Amnro for R$76.5 million (a direct sale by the State, rather than by the Federal authorities) and
Banco do Estado do Amazonas; sold to Bradesco for R$182.9 million. With a limited supply of
banks remaining to be privatized, future transactions of banks deemed to have strategic value,
such as that of the Banco do Estado de Santa Catarina (BESC), may become more expensive.
However, attention is also likely to turn increasingly towards small private banks.
15 Details of the launching of the privatization process and huge difficulties encountered are discussed in Harry
Makler (2000) "Bank Transformation and Privatization in Brazil" Conference on Bank Privatization, Federal
Reserve Bank of Dallas / World Bank.
16 Private bank acquisition premia also rose, as observed in the purchases of Banco Real by ABN-Amro,
Bandeirantes by Unibanco and Boavista by Bradesco.

49. The privatization of state banks is slowly coming to an end: Four state banks remain to be
sold, and the process is expected to be concluded by mid-2002. This final wave of privatizations
is not expected to generate the level of enthusiasm seen in the past. Three of four remaining state
banks are in remote and, for the most part, poor northeastern states, with little strategic value for
the established players who often do not even have a presence in those areas. Nevertheless, it is
expected that large private banks such as Bradesco and Itau will bid in most, if not all of the
auctions, for purely defensive reasons, i.e., to keep out competitors from those markets. An
example of this can be seen by the latest acquisition of The State Bank of Amazonas (BEA) by
Bradesco on January 24, 2002. Whilst Itau had qualified for the privatization process, it did not
compete in the auction.

50. It is possible that the Central Bank will once more have to use incentives to facilitate the
sale of most of these banks, including exemptions from reserve requirements, the use of
privatization currencies, and the inclusion of tax credits. The only exception is likely to be Banco
do Estado de Santa Catarina (BESC), with a market share of 30 percent in the state of Santa
Catarina, 600,000 clients, 256 branches and 529 service outlets, R$1.8 billion in assets and
R$661 million in equity. BESC will be privatized by the Federal Government after injecting
R$1.25 billion in Federal Government paper to cover its bad loans. While this bank is much
smaller than Banestado or Banespa, it is the largest of the remaining banks, and it is located in
the wealthy Southern part of the country. The auction of BESC is therefore likely to be heavily
contested, with few, if any, sweeteners from the government.

Annex 3: Access to Financial Services
ANNEX 3: ACCESS TO FINANCIAL SERVICES'
1.     Analysis of financial access is a relatively new concept, and as ongoing analyses reveal, it
is a mulitidimensional issue. According to many measures of financial access, Brazil is not
obviously lagging in terms of its provision of financial services, compared to other developing
countries, especially those in the Latin America region. Yet there is considerable scope for it to
better its performance. On the one side, the Brazilian financial system is the largest in the region
and offers a broad variety of services to its clients. Yet, on the other side, the absolute numbers
of Brazilians without access to financial services is large. As discussed below (Part I), financial
access is low in Brazil relative to its potential. Authorities in Brazil are very aware of the
challenge of expanding the provision of financial services, and a number of recent measures have
been taken to increase access (Part II). But a number of challenges lie ahead, which could require
the exploration of new incentives, new institutional arrangements, and new infrastructure for
Brazil's financial system (Part IH).
CURRENT LEVELS OF ACCESS TO FINANCIAL SERVICES
Access - Depth of Intermediation
2.     Looking first at the overall depth of financial intermediation, Brazil is an average
performer. Although Brazil does better than its large Latin American neighbors such as Mexico
and Argentina, also deemed to be among the middle income countries of the region, it can also
be pointed out that Brazil performs poorly compared to the emerging markets of East Asia; many
with a lower per capita income. Extending the comparators to include more developed countries
it is clear that Brazil has a much lower overall share of credit to the private sector by financial
intermediaries to GDP than countries such as the US or UK (Figure A3.1; Table A3.1).

Access - Regional Distribution of Financial Services and Bank Branch Density
3.    It is however uncontestable that access to financial services is unevenly distributed
geographically, with sparse services in the poorer parts of the country. Evidence on the skewed
regional distribution of financial services in the country is presented in Tables A3.2 and A3.3
below, which show that banking facilities are concentrated in large southeastern states. Around
57 percent of all municipalities in the Northeast and in the North and nearly 20 percent of the
population in these states have no access to a bank branch. Nationwide, 30 percent of all
municipalities and 6 percent of the population, have no access to formal financial services. There
are seven states (out of 27), mostly in the North, where the share of municipalities with no bank
branch is over 70 percent. About 1,680 poorer and arguably less populated municipalities, out of
a total of some 5,600 municipalities, have not a single bank branch.
4.     Even when bank branches are present, services are more constrained in bank branches in
poorer regions. Each bank branch in the North and Northeast of Brazil has to serve nearly three
times as many clients as in the South. However, this is also explained in part by lower overall
levels of economic activity in these regions. Although each branch has to serve almost three
times as many clients, GDP per bank branch in the North is less than twice as high as in the
South. (Table A3.2).

6.     But comparisons with other developing countries suggest that Brazil is not obviously
under-branched (Table A3.4). Brazil has more bank branches relative to population than other
Latin American countries and India and Indonesia, two large developing countries. Measured
relative to GDP, its bank branch intensity is lower than Colombia, India and Indonesia, which
are all poorer than Brazil, but the two latter are much more densely populated. Branch intensity
relative to GDP in Brazil is higher than in Mexico or Chile, although both are richer than Brazil.

7.     Measured according to average geographic area covered per branch, Brazil appears at
first to be under-banked relative to all other comparator developing countries except for Chile.
However, this is easily explained by the low population density of Brazil and the vast areas that
are not populated. An econometric estimation of predicted branch intensity compared to actual
intensity for Brazil and other countries, correcting for differences in branch intensity explained
by differences in GDP, total population, and geographic size finds that Brazil is the only
Annex 3 Page 4 of 19



Annex 3: Access to Financial Services
developing economy that is not under-banked by this standard, since its actual branch intensity is
higher than predicted.
8.    Banks' decisions to open or maintain branches are affected by the high fixed costs
involved as well as restrictive regulations on labor and security. Recognizing this, in the last one
year, authorities have passed new regulations on correspondent banking (discussed further in
Part II below), which enable the provision of banking services with fewer restrictive regulatory
requirements. Many banks are beginning to provide banking services to low income segments
through such correspondent services or service outposts, provided at outlets such as post offices,
shop or supermarket chains, and in the case of one bank, through a lottery chain. Caixa
Econ6mica Federal, which runs the Casa Loterica chain, estimates that the average cost of a bank
transaction at a correspondent bank is only a fourth as much as at a branch. Banks also provide
services via ATM machines, via the internet, and over the phone. Some of these are discussed
further below. Measuring banking services provision through bank branches alone thus fails to
capture the full range of vehicles for delivery of financial services. Data so far indicate that
public and foreign banks rely more on service outposts than private domestic banks, whereas
private domestic banks rely more on ATMs.

New Technologiesfor Access -Automatic TeUler Machines
9.    Access to automatic teller machines has increased substantially over the last five years
(from about 5,000 in 1996 to more than 16,000 in 2001) in all regions of Brazil, though service
may again be biased in favor of the southern regions.2 In terms of numbers of ATMs, fifty-nine
percent are in the Southeast, compared to only four percent in the North. In terms of ATMs per
inhabitant, the Southeast, Center-West and South of Brazil are clearly the best served. But
adjusting for differences in levels of economic activity, regional differentials are less distinct.
The Northeast of Brazil actually has a fairly high density of ATMs relative to GDP per capita,
while ratios for the South and Center-West are low. However, the Southeast is still clearly the
best off region, even adjusting for income differentials, while the North is clearly the worst off.

institutions in Brazil.

New Technologies for Access - Phone and Internet
10.    Brazil's large banks have substantially endeavored to expand internet usage among their
clients. All of Brazil's largest banks have developed extensive internet-based activities, and
comparisons with Asia's emerging economies based on survey evidence suggest that a higher
percentage of users in Brazil make extensive use of new technologies (Figure A3.4). Experts
believe that the number of internet users in Brazil will double by 2005.

11.    Brazil's banks are even succeeding in closing the gap with the USA in terms of internet
penetration among clients (Figure A3.5). It appears that whereas the use of computers and the
internet in Brazil for recreational and leisure activities is lower than in the USA, for purposes of
work and including services such as banking, Brazil scores better than the USA, at least among
the connected segment of the population.

12.    Brazil's banks and banking authorities recognize that service delivery through these
mechanisms can be highly cost effective. The Central Bank of Brazil has estimated that internet
is the cheapest channel to deliver financial services, followed by ATMs, telephones and finally
branches. Its estimates suggest that, in 2001, the average banking transaction cost using internet
was R$0.24 (US$10 cents), compared to R$0.33 (US$13 cents) for telephone dial-in banking
services and R$2 (US$80 cents) for transactions at a branch. Precise estimates vary by source,
but all confirm huge savings in using alternative delivery channels. The majority of Brazilian
banks do not charge clients for using the internet to carry out their banking transactions. Some
also offer subsidized financing for the purchase of personal computers. Moreover, the phone
industry is quite competitive, and cellular phone charges have fallen significantly over the last
few years.

13.    However, large disparities in access to internet and phone services are recognized to
exist, which have limited the use of these technologies to extend financial services to the poorer
segments of the population. An estimated 90 percent of the 8 million intemet banking users
belong to high income classes. Only about 4 percent of total individual bank clients used intemet
banking in 1999 and only 10 percent of corporate users. Access is in part limnited by supply, as
the smaller Brazilian banks do not have websites, but also due to low connectivity, especially in
rural areas, although growing rapidly.
Annex 3 Page 7 of 19



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
14.    The telephone may allow banks to penetrate better lower segments. Surveys indicate that
a much wider range of persons (in terms of per-capita income) owns cell phones than computers,
and the cost of a cellular phone call is much cheaper than in many other countries in the world
(Figure A3.6). However cellular phone call charges remain much higher than fixed line call
charges and in addition, the subscriber must buy the phone itself, and pay an activation fee which
is high relative to per capita income.
Access - Availability of Financial Services for SmaU Entrepreneurs
15.    The skewed distribution of financial services in Brazil extends to the relative scarcity of
capital for small and medium size enterprises. Some studies on growth constraints of small and
medium enterprises show that 54 percent of the entrepreneurs of this segment indicate that lack
of financing was the main impediment for their expansion. This is a particularly important issue
in Brazil, given the enormous representation of this segment especially in terms of numbers of
establishments and in employment. In 1994 SMEs were estimated to represented 99 percent of
all enterprises; they employed around 59 percent of the labor force and produced 41.1 percent of
the income of manufacturing industry.3 Looking at microenterprises alone, as many as 90 percent
of all firms in Brazil belong to this category.4 Micro-enterprises account for 24 percent of
employment and about 13 percent of GDP. Among these, firms with up to 4 employees account
for 57 percent of all firms in industry, 82 percent in commerce and 74 percent in services. In
addition, there are an estimated 14 million informal micro and small enterprises in Brazil.5

16.   A second source of information on the degree to which enterprises in Brazil and other
countries rate financing as an operational and growth constraint suggests that specific constraints
such as high interest rates and collateral requirements may be more important in Brazil than the
absolute availability of credit, although that too is an issue. But Brazilian enterprises do not rate
the financing of investment as more of an operational and growth constraint than entrepreneurs
in other Latin American and upper-middle income countries. However, they rate high interest
rates and collateral requirements as greater obstacles than enterprises in comparator countries
(Table A3.6).

17.    Separating firms on the basis of size reveals fairly similar patterns by size group. While
medium-sized firms seem to be most constrained in their financing, especialy in terms of access
to long-term loans, compared to both small and large firms, most of the differences across the
groups are not statistically significant. For firms of all sizes, high interest rates are the single
most important impediment.

Access - Availability of Financial Servicesfor Small Depositors and Borrowers
18.   The supply of banking services to small depositors and small borrowers provides an
indirect indication of the extent to which poorer households and small entrepreneurs have access
to financial services. Looking at average loan and deposit sizes for banks of different ownership

groups, findings suggest that private banks, both domestic and foreign, show larger average loan
sizes and larger average time deposits than public banks (Tables A3.8).6 Thus public banks

19.   Examining loans and deposits by size category, data show that public banks lend a
larger share of their total loan portfolio to small borrowers than private and foreign banks (12.9
percent versus 5.3 percent), and tend to have somewhat higher proportions of their total client
base among small borrowers (68 percent versus 56 percent for private domestic and 64 percent
for private foreign banks). There are no significant differences in the size distribution of demand
and savings deposits, while public banks do have a greater share of total time deposits in small
time deposits. In terms of small depositors, the public banks'advantages are less clear. In terms
of the number of small demand or time accounts relative to the total number of time accounts,
banks of different ownershlip do not differ dramatically, although in terms of value of small time
deposits, the share of federal banks is higher - ie, private banks have smaller average balances in
these accounts than public banks. Thus it appears that private banks offer deposit taking services
to small clients, but more limited lending services (Table A3.9). And among the private banks,
analysis shows that the largest among these focus most on the small depositor and borrower.
Many of the small private banks seem to have a niche function, serving a small group of
relatively large clients.

Annex 3: Access to Financial Services
RECENT INITIATIVES TO EXPAND ACCESS
20.   The government has begun taking a number of important steps to extend the frontier of
financial services towards the underserved population. These span a wide range of reforms;
more enforceable credit contracts, and enhanced debtor information through the expansion of
services offered by the Central Bank's Central do Risco are crucial in this respect. These efforts
have been documented elsewhere and thus the present document focuses principally on measures
so far less discussed in the Bank, which have been taken towards the expansion of micro and
small scale lending institutions, and the expansion of service points - such as post offices,
supermarkets, and lottery points - to make financial services available to population in remote
and poorer areas. It has also eased restrictions to widen the membership for cooperatives while,
at the same time, enhancing prudential oversight of cooperatives, via delegated supervision to
cooperative associations (which are directly supervised by the Central Bank). A selection of
these measures are detailed further below.
(i) New Regulations for Microfinance Expansion , the Communidade SolidAria
21.   There has been a dramatic improvement in the legal framework for microenterprises over
the last five years, which has been brought about by a multilateral dialogue between the
government and civil society, organized under the auspices of the Council of Community
Solidarity ("Conselho da Comunidade Soliddria"). The Council, which developed under the
patronage and effective leadership of the First Lady in the present Cardoso government regime,
was established in 1995 to promote citizen participation in political debate on social themes, and
to define priorities and social actions to fight poverty and social exclusion in Brazil. Micro-credit
has been a key theme in the Comunidade Soliddria's rounds of political debate ("Rodadas de
Interlocu,do Polftica"), first initiated in 1997-1998. These debates have led to the
implementation of several actions by the government and the National Monetary Council, with
important results for the development of micro-credit in Brazil.
22.   Changes in the legal framework which for the first time gave effective legitimacy to
microcredit in Brazil were introduced with Quinta Rodada de Interlocu,do Poiftica (1997-98),
devoted to the potential role of micro-credit in Brazil, which concluded that micro-credit could
play a strategic role in terms of employment and income distribution, but that its expansion
required a different treatment from traditional credit operations. It was recognized that the
operations of micro-credit institutions were restricted by several impediments, pertaining mostly
to the legal framework.
23.   A commission was then created with representatives of the Central Bank, BNDES and
the Ministry of Finance with the objective of presenting a proposal for changes in the regulation
of micro-credit institutions. Prior to 1999, microfinance could only be undertaken by a non-profit
organization (ONG, or Organiza(4o Ndo Governamental), and these were constrained by the one
percent per month ceiling on interest rates imposed by Brazil's usury law, which has been
meaningless in Brazil's high inflation periods, and is also limiting in the case of microfinance
loans, which tradtionally in most countries are often for periods of weeks or months, and at
higher interest rates than loans in the formal and large scale sectors. NGOs were also
constrained in terms of access to capital, to grants or credit lines from government or foreign
donors. The work of this commission led to major legal changes in 1999, with the introduction of
Annex 3 Page 11 of 19



Brazil: Proposed Second Financial Sector Adjustment Loan
new legal forms of entities with considerably more flexibility in terms of both funding and
lending, for the extension of microcredit. The two new institutional forms created were (i) the
OSCIPs (Organiza,do da Sociedade Civil de Interesse Publico) or Public Interest Civil
Societies, and (ii) SCMs (Sociedades de Credito ao Microempreendedor) or Microenterprise
Credit Societies.7 Importantly, both organizational forms were simultaneously exempted from
the restrictions of the Usury law.8
24.    The first of these organizational forms, the OSCIPs, can have .a range of objectives,
which are not limited to microfinance; OSCIPs exist in many fields such as science, education,
research, etc. Like NGOs, they are deemed to be not-for-profit organizations. Importantly,
OSCIPs can 'sign Cooperation Agreements with the public sector to implement activities and
projects of public interest', which implies that they could have access to public sector funds, in
addition to donor funds and owner/sponsor funds. However, OSCIPs are not able to access
funds from financial institutions or to mobilize deposits from the public. OSCIPs are subject to
some reporting requirements to the Ministry of Justice, but do not have any prudential
regulations such as minimum capital requirements, which may apply to financial institutions.
25.    The second institutional form created, the SCM, is much closer to a microcredit society,
as they exist in many other countries.9 SCMs are more formal and are specialised finanicial
entities regulated by the Central Bank of Brazil. They are deemed to be for-profit entities. In
terms of access to support or finance, SCMs can take loans or lines of credit from foreign or
domestic financial institutions, and in February 2001 their access to funding was further
widened, together with permission to use instruments such as fiduciary alienation for extending
credit.'0 However, issuing debt or mobilizing deposits remain restricted. Subsequent regulations
issued in July 2001 specified operational limits on caital, leverage and risk; SCMs are required to
comply with minimum start-up capital, ongoing capital adequacy and limits on risk
concentration. The regulations issued in July 2001 also permit SCMs, like banks, to operate
through Microcredit Service Points (Posto de Atendimento de Microcr6dito), and require them to
submit information on their lending operations to the Central Bank's credit risk center - the
Central de Risco de Credito - which in principle could permit them in the future to obtain greatly
enhanced credit information on prospective clients. l
7 Law 9790 of March 23, 1999, which came to be popularly known as the New Law of the Tertiary Sector,
established the OSCIP. Decree No 3100 of June 30, 1999 provided details on its authorization, functioning and
supervision. Reporting requirements are minimal, but for lines of credit through public entities, external audits are
required. For details see especially 'OSCIP - Organizacdo du Sociedade Civil de Interesse Publico - A Lei 9790/99
como Alternativa para o Terceiro Setor. Comunidade Solidaria. Brasilia, July 2001.
8 Medida Provis6ria 1914-4 of June 28, 1999 excluded both OSCIPs and SCMs from the Usury law.

established reporting requirements to the Central Bank as well as public disclosure requirements for financial

No. 2898 of the Central Bank of February29, 2000).
10 Law 10194 of February 2001 extended funding sources to include the SEBRAE, and also, companies registered

Resolution 2874 of the National Monetary Council of July 26, 2001.

Annex 3: Access to Financial Services
26.   These new institutional forms are clearly a major step forward for microcredit in Brazil,
although the paucity of SCMs to date suggests that there is capacity for further adjustment and
facilitation. Some existing NGOs which are microfinance institutions in practice are considering
modifying their legal framework in order to benefit from the ability to tap external sources of
finance, including in some cases splitting up existing organizations into two entities, an NGO
rendering non-financial services (training and consulting) and an SCM for all financial
operations. This would expand the sources of funding these entities have access to, thereby
removing constraints to their expansion in recent years. Some factoring companies are also
considering or undergoing conversion to SCMs. By October 2001, around six SCMs had been
established and another half dozen applications were pending approval.
27.   The recent modifications in 2001 of the regulations for SCMs were the outcome of
further debate on the expansion of micro-credit in Brazil, which resumed intensively in the latest
Round of the Comunidade Soliddria for the years 2001-2002. In March 2001 four working
groups were established, to discuss specific problem areas in micro-credit - the legal framework,
information and institutional support, training, and performance evaluations. The working group
on the Legal Framework finalized its proposals in May 2001, and modifications to the
regulations on SCMs were introduced in July 2001 were based on these preliminary findings. In
October 2001, all the working groups on microfinance debated their results with a cross section
of government and civil society interests in microfinance. 12
28.   Changes implemented to date have focused largely on measures which would be easy to
implement without the need for Congressional approval - that is, short of new laws. Further
changes are now being debated, which would however require legal amendments. These
include, permitting deposit taking through savings mobilization and a simplified tax framework.
Draft laws have already been prepared regarding some of these points, and sub-working groups
have been established to discuss them further.
(ii) Correspondent Banking - the Postal System and Other Networks
29.   Since March 2000, banks have been authorized to establish 'correspondent banking'
agreements with other (non-financial) legal entities such as convenience shops, supermarket
chains, etc, and most recently, post offices; for the provision of basic financial transactions to a
wider public (payment transactions, deposits). Other transactions may be allowed, but require a
specific authorization from the Central Bank. Many banks have now begun to take advantage of
this new legislation to penetrate underserved areas at a lower cost.'3 The cost advantages of the
correspondent system are huge in Brazil, where the fixed costs of establishing a bank branch are
raised disproportionally by a series of security regulations, labor laws, restrictive hours, etc.
30.   One of the most significant events to follow this regulation was the creation of a national
chain of financial service correspondent outposts through links with the postal system, through
12 Summarized in a document entitled "The Expansion of Micro-credit in Brazil" ("A Expansdo do Microcr6dito no

Solidaria, as part of the AAA task on Access to Financial Services.

the auction by the government, in August 2001 of the postal franchise. It was awarded to Brazil's
largest private bank, Bradesco, for a price of R$201 millions (US$81 million), which won the
exclusive right to distribute financial services through the 5,532 agencies of the Brazilian post
office, ECT (Empresa Brasileira de Correios e Telhgrafos). The contract has a maturity of five
years, which begins when each post office agency will be connected to Bradesco. All agencies
are expected to be operational by 2003. The concession will therefore last at least un.til 2008. It
can be renewed without a fresh bidding process. By the end of 2001, 1000 post office agencies
were operational. In 2002, 1,600 additional agencies will be connected and the rest are expected
to be connected in 2003. Bradesco has undertaken to connect agencies located in municipalities
without banking service on a priority basis.'4 ECT has a greater relative presence in the poor
Northeast area of Brazil than the banks. Bradesco will be allowed to distribute basic financial
services through the ECT such as payment and account transaction services, in exclusivity. The
post office is likely to be present in the 1680 municipalities which lack formal financial services.

million current account holders to 15.2 million. Through the post office, Bradesco intends to
target low income individuals, especially in remote areas, which otherwise could not be targeted

32.   Caixa, the giant public sector bank, has a monopoly franchise on the network of about
8,000 loto game (Casa Loterica) outlets spread out across the country. In addition, Caixa is
establishing new relationships with 2,400 correspondents, with the objective of being present in
all 2,000 municipalities in which it is currently absent. As a result, by June 2002, Caixa should
be present in all municipalities of Brazil with at least one correspondent banker offering payment
and deposit services. Payment transactions worth R$65 million are made each year through
Caixa's loto game outlets, of which 55 are worth less than R$40 each (US$16).
33.   With the ECT network of Bradesco, and Caixa's loto game (Casa Lotefica) outlet
franchise, it is aimed that all municipalities in Brazil will have access to at least one bank, and
sometimes two, for basic financial transactions (payment, and account deposits and transfers),
when these two networks are fully operational. Both institutions plan to extend the services
offered by these networks to opening of accounts and credit granting, but several identification
and security issues still need to be solved.
34.   Other large banks in Brazil are also increasingly establishing associations with local
distributors to reach out to the masses. Unibanco for instance has acquired a specialty finance
company Fininvest, and has signed agreements with the supermarket chains Magazine Luisa and
Globex's Ponto Frio by which Fininvest employees can distribute consumer loans to customers
in the stores. Through these three outlets, Unibanco reaches some seven million people of the
lower middle and low income groups, mostly in the South and Southeast of Brazil.
(iii) Enhanced SmalU Savings Facilities - Tesouro Direto
35.   A significant development in the expansion of services for savers is the recent decision of
the government to allow individual small savers to purchase government bonds, through the
intemet, under its new Treasury Direct program. The initiative is pioneering in Latin America,
although well known in countries such as Spain and in the United States, where ten percent of
government debt is in the hands of small investors. Closely modeled upon the program of the
same name in the USA, and widely used in a number of OECD countries, such retail debt sales
programs have the double advantage of allowing a safe savings vehicle for small savers,
remunerated at market rates, while at the same time, allowing the government more efficient
mechanisms for tapping local debt markets.15 Issues began on January 7, 2002, and are available
24 hours a day, 7 days a week, cleared through the Companhia Brasileira de LiquidaVdo e de
Cust6dia - CBLC. Prior to this, only banks, bond dealers and other financial institutions
registered in the Sistema Especial de Liquidacdo e Cust6dia, (SELIC) with a minimum amount
of R$50,000 could purchase government bonds.
15 The Brazilian Treasury (Tesouro Nacional), http://www.tesouro.fazenda.gov.br/tesouro_nacional on December
12th, 2001 issued Ordinance n° 554, which approved the regulation of the public offering of bonds to individuals via
the internet and on offers began on January 7.

(iv) Loans for Small Entrepreneurs - The Sebrae Program
41.   SEBRAE ("Servi,o Brasileiro de Apoio as Micro e Pequenas Empresas" -Brazilian
Services to Support Micro and Small Enterprises-) is a government agency which was originally
set up to promote the development of microenterprises, through technical assistance programs to
promote new micro and small enterprises and strengthen existing ones.'6 SEBRAE has one office
in each of Brazil's 27 states, with a total staff of 4,500 persons and more than 500 points of
contact. The local offices have a large degree of autonomy, under the coordination of a central
unit in Brasilia which establishes norms and strategies for the group. SEBRAE's budget comes
from a 0.3 precent contribution raised on companies' payroll. In 1999, it's budget totaled
US$450 million.
42.   SEBRAE's support to microfinance began with its Loan Guarantee Fund for Micro and
Small Enterprises; FAMPE (Fundo de Aval as Microempresas e Empresas de Pequeno Porte),
available however only for loans issued by some public banks with which SEBRAE has
negotiated specific agreements (Banco do Brasil, Caixa Econ8mica Federal, Banco do Nordeste,
Banco da Amaz6nia and Banco de Desenvolvimento do Estado de Minas Gerais). The guarantee
is provided for loans financing long term investments. Working capital loans are accepted only
if they complement a long term financing loan and represent less than 50 percent of the total.
43.   In July 1999, in parallel to the government's efforts to launch new institutional forms for
microcredit, SEBRAE defined a new strategic plan focused on programs aimed at eliminating
restrictions to access to credit by micro and small enterprises. Its Venture Capital Program,
(Programa de Capital de Risco -PCR) launched shortly after, was focused essentially on enabling
the implementation of Law 10194 of February 2001, which permitted expanded access to
funding for OSCIPs and SCMs.17 It allows SEBRAE to participate in venture capital funds
investing in emerging micro and small companies with a high growth potential, particularly those
with a technological basis or a potential to export. SEBRAE can only take minority stakes (10
percent and 33.3 percent) and must accompany such participation with technical and institutional
advice. This program is highly complementary to SEBRAE's existing support to incubators.
44.   Shortly after, in October 2001, as part of the events surrounding the summit of the
Communidade Solidaria, SEBRAE expanded its services to include special programs of credit
for microenterprises. Its new program aims specifically at OSCIPs (Organiza,do da Sociedade
Civil de Interesse Publico), but also encompasses SCMs as needed. The new scheme plans to
16 SEBRAE was established by law (Laws 8029 and 8154) in 1990.
17 See footnote 10 above.
